DEPARTMENT, the Company is now supplying FISH

A telegraph order from any country town despatched
before 1 p.m. (provided the Telegraph Department do
their part) is certain of execution by mail train same day,
and as Fish is carried at half parcels rates— equal to about
id- per lb. for the riiost distant towns—the luxury of a
fish diet is within the reach of all, the F.F. & I. Co.'s
prices (free on rail) being extremely moderate. We pre-
pay trainage, thereby effecting a saving of 50 per cent.
The Company stock all kinds of New Zealand and N.S,
Wales fish, also some American kinds—notably Salmon—
and arrangements have been concluded with the Orient
Line to fit a steamer. The first consignment of English
fish and game leaves London sth May, 1893.
Price-lists obtainable at Company’s Depot, 135 King-st.,
or from the Manager, 25 to 33 Harbour-street.
Note,— We shall be pleased for country visitors to in-
spect our refrigerating establishment at Harbour-street,
The Company deliver daily to all parts of the city and
suburbs—Milk, Ice, Cream, Butter, Poultry, Pish, Game,
Pastry, &c.

THE
NAMBROK ESTATE,
situated on the Latrobe River, Cippsland
comprises 15,000 acres of very rich land
which can scarcely be surpassed for agri-
cultural, fattening and dairy purposes. The
land is specially adapted for the growth of
maize, hops, root crops and cereals, and is
practically ready for the plough. Th&
Nambrok sheep and cattle have been for
many years noted for the excellence and
high prices obtained. The rainfall is abun-
dant, averaging about 30 inches per annum,
and the climate salubrious, being free from
extreme heat and cold. An Unlimited
supply of water can be obtained at a mode-
rate depth, under 30 feet, in any portion of
the Estate.
The Thompson River, which is close to
the northern boundary of the property, can
be easily and cheaply utilised for irrigation'
purposes. The Rosedale Railway Station,
111 miles from Melbourne, is within 2|
miles of the property, and the railway line
runs directly through the property, and
the Heyfield station on the Maffra line is
about 5 miles distant.
THE
MELTON PARK ESTATE
comprises 7146 acres, close to Bacchus
Marsh, in the Parish of Yancardook,
County of Bourke, and is well known ip
Victoria for its great fertility and resi-
dential attractions. It is about a couple of
miles from the Melton Railway Station,
20 miles distant from Melbourne, where ap
ever ready market exists for everything
the Estate produces.
Plans ofhothEstateswill he forwarded
on application.

A London Panic in ’70.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
France was daring Germany to tread on
the tails of its Napoleonic eagles. The London
summer was choky and sweaty, but the London
Stock Exchange rules requiring all its visitants to
wear belltoppers remained inexorable. (Any un-
orthodox hat is promptly footballed by the
“gentlemen” of the Exchange.) Likewise, the
etiquette of black-cloth frock-coats was in force.
The huge building that forms the head-quarters
of what is generally known as the Money Market
is situated in the centre of an irregular triangle,
bounded by Bartholomew Lane (where, from the
opposite side of the street the Bank of England
frowns on its main entrance, Capel Court), Throg-
morton-street, and Threadneedle-street and its
continuation. Daily meeting within its walls are
some 1000 members and clerks. Gorgeously-
attired waiters resplendent in black-cloth liveries,
with red-cloth facings, gold-lace streaks and brass-
buttons, are seated inside its portals, to prevent
the entrance of any save “members” of the
“House,” or those of their clerks who have
passed the ordeal imposed by the “Committee,”
or the specially-registered telegraph-messengers.
On 19th July, ’7O, the simmering distrust in
London financial circles reached its climax. On
that date, France declared war against Prussia.
I visited the “ Market ” that morning, telling
a liveried Cerberus my business. I was allowed to
stand alongside this gorgeous creature’s seat,
while he yelled in stentorian tones —“ Smith !
Smith! Jones! Jones! A. B. Smith! C. D.
Jones!”
Other criers took up the names, until Jones,
flurried and heated, slipped for a moment from
his “ market,” only to inform me that he could not
leave. Would I wait? He would send Smith.
Consols had dropped three-eighths; and, if war
was declared, they’d go down another one per
cent. Rails were down two per cent, all round.
And then he rushed madly inside again.
Suddenly an irresistible gust of new-comers
swept into the building. Amid a babel roar, the
ear could catch spurts of intelligibility—“ Sell
20,000 at a quarter ! Sell 40,000 at an eighth !
Sell 50,000 at a sixteenth ! ”
A sudden lull.
“ War has been declared.”
Then a rush to the doors for newspapers.
Presently, a circle was formed under the great
dome, and a well-trained voice was heard enthusi-
astically singing “The Marseillaise,” the chorus
of which was excitedly taken up by a thousand
throats.
Then the pent-up feelings of the stock-gamblers
broke loose ! Men heretofore supposed to be hard-
headed and level-brained jumped on their hats
savagely, wrung their hands despairingly, plucked
at their beards, and yelled like chained lunatics 1
Frantic apparitions dashed hither and thither,
screaming—“Sell, sell!”
Three o’clock struck. The watchmen at each
door sprung their rattles, men lighted cigars and
calmed down, and business yawned. Four o'clock,
and business was over for the day.
Men worth thousands in the morning went
home ruined.
» # •
A few days later was the fortnightly
settling-day. .
Business was noisily humming. Eleven o clock
struck. There was a hush. The head-waiter rose
in his place against the great pillar, almost in the
centre of the building. All eyes were fixed on
him, tall, erect, white-headed, clean-shaven,, and
with an irreproachable clean collar showing in
contrast to the old-fashioned black “stock.” He
had removed his hat. He tapped once with a
wooden hammer —the hush became stiller ; twice
—it was as the sibilance of a collective whisper j
thrice —it was absolute and painful.
“Gentlemen, Mr. A. B. Smith begs to inform
the House that he cannot comply with his
b3ir^9iinBt //
Amid dead silence, a similar formula was gone
through with C. D. Jones, E. F. Browne, G. H.
Robinson, right up to Y. Z. Fit^Fortescue.
Then , there was a mad stir, and a rushing
around of wild-eyed men to see how they stood
with the defaulters, and a second edition of the
post-Marsellaise agony was enacted,
• • •
A few weeks, however, settled that panic.
Crushed France and victorious Germany both
ordered their chiefest supplies from British manu-
facturers, and freetraders claimed the local revival
of commerce as due to their own vaunted policy !
But supposing that Napoleon the Little had had
the first luck of Napoleon the Great, and Britain
had been herself embroiled in war ?
VECTIS.
“ The people are not fickle, but persistent
in their attachments.” — London Spectator.
Dear Bulletin, —You are right about
Westralia being a howling wilderness ; only it is
“more sc.” Yet it has had every chance.
Founded in ’37 under most favourable circum-
stances, its settlers, some 20 years later, petitioned
England for convict labour—and got it. If its
“naturally” watered country were half as good
as (say) the Queensland mulga lands, there would
now be, by natural process of increase, many
millions of sheep on it; there are actually s ume
1 (500,000. The land is so poor that enough wheat
for home consumption can’t be grown on it, and
the settlers have to import even bacon, cheese, i
butter, eggs. &c. The farmers are not lazy, labour
is cheap—l have seen good men working hard-
-408. a month—but the land is just what a Yankee
cynic once described it, “the finest country in
the world to, run through an hour-glass.” A while
back hundreds of visitors arrived monthly, and
their £2O or so apiece boomed the small community
a bit • but no fool is fool enough to stay here if he
can help it. I advise those who mean to give
Westralia a trial to keep enough cash in hand
always to take them out of it when they want to
leave: again, as they are sure to do, or they may
get stranded here for ever.—Yours, Mat. the
Drummer.
The respective values of dailies and
weeklies as advertising mediums is being discussed as a
result of the rapid decline of the weekly of
dailies. Once upon a time, in the halcyon days of Cobb s
coaches, people in the country were content to receive
their news weekly; but the railways and the telegraph
have altered all that. The country resident is no longer
satisfied with a weekly paper containing a great mass of
matter cribbed from some daily journal printed at the
same office. He has mostly read the daily paper and is
as disgusted with having it reserved to him under
another name as a man would be who was offeied hash at
a first-class hotel. If he takes a .weekly paper ■, he wants
to tret one like The Bulletin, origins and readable fiom
cover to cover. There can be no doubt that the doom of
these weekly editions of daily papers is sealed. I cope
don’t give a rap for ponderous co lections of stale
news pitchforked out of the daily editions and jumbled
nn with some “agricultural notes about how to kilt
vermin on cabbages. They have ceased to read these weekly
editions. Only the advertisers, attracted and deluded hj
the enormous circulation usually
faithful. Even they are beginning to find no results,
and are dropping out.

But do you use SAPOLIO ? If you don’t you are as much behind the
age as if you cut grass with a dinner knife. Once there were no soaps
Then one soap served all purposes. Now the sensible folks use one soap

tor all scouring and house-cleaning.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twentu
Shillings sterling — with, postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. Tn that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maclkod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
iength, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when’ the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

SATURDAY, JUNE 10, 1893.
The “ New Australia” Madness.
A few days hence a couple of hundred
persons will set sail from Port Jackson
in an old timber-boat and brave the
raging main in the endeavour to reach
Paraguay. The departure of the Royal
Tar with the first fatuous batch, their
families and belongings, only waits final
arrangements. On the assumption that life
among wild oranges and yerha mate scrub
has capabilities which it does not offer in
Australia, one of the most feather-headed
expeditions ever conceived since Ponce de
Leon started out to find the Fountain of
Eternal Youth, or Sir Galahad pursued the
Holy Grail, is actually about to set forth.
It is even worse than either of these
escapades, for De Leon could look forward to
a good deal of incidental looting and pillag-
ing, while Galahad was compelled to keep
up appearances somehow. The New Aus-
tralians have no hope before them at best
but to work hard, under great and admitted
disadvantages, for a living which, if they
get it at all, will be rough and meagre for
some years. How far emotion can cloud
reason a thousand fervid and fanatical pro-
jects of this kind have attested in the past.
But this is a crowning instance. A large
number of people, sane so far as theii past
record shows, and capable of exercising their
judgment as adults, have decided to woik
together on a basis of perfect equality. And
they have chosen Paraguay to do it in !
* # *
Or, rather, it should be said they are 1 ‘going
blind,” and Paraguay happens to have been
chosen for them. The scouts of the promoters
of this insane exodus were let loose generally
upon the waste lands and thorny places of
South America. When they were exploring
‘be death-stricken solitudes of the Lower
i-geutine the Rio Negro was proclaimed a
i n ei o Eden by the official organ of the
srrsk, A o?°z ™
J cae Aijdine pampas, or
disputed the possibility of setting up a
paradise of communal industry thereabout
was nob thought to be of much account.
After awhile the prospectors gave that part
of the continent best, thus endorsing the
opinions of those hero who know anything
about it. Then some of the promoters
wanted to know who ever said anything
about going to Rio Negro, or word's to that
effect. In the most disingenuous way they
sought to convey the idea that the formation
of a colony there had never been contem-
plated. in Paraguay the prospectors found
grass, water, trees a thousand miles from the
nearest timber-market, an affable Govern-
ment eager to get them at any price, and
ready to promise anything, and an idle and
nerveless native population. That is really
all that their report discloses. On the
strength of it, of a grant of land, and
certain concessions on paper, the Royal Tar
is to sail with a load of communal enthusiasts
who might as well be bound for the South
Pole, or the off-side of the moon, so far as
many of them are aware. ,
# # »
One need not look beyond the. proposed
basis of settlement and action to see the
hopelessness of the scheme. It involves
equal division of all products. The New
Australian who cannot, or will not, do one-
quarter as much work as his mate will, under
this interpretation of “ mateship,” be en-
titled to draw just as much from the pooled
results. “ A man may produce a loaf of
bread or a painting,” says the chief Sydney
agent of this abnormal fiasco, “ but there
will be no difference in the remuneration for
his labour.” Or, to pub it another way, a
man may do a loaf or work his hardest, and
it will be all the same on pay-day. Such a
system may promote brotherhood, but it
will be of the Cain and Abel quality, not of
the kind the New Australians go so far to
seek. If the strong and capable and willing
among these pioneers wish to contribute to
the support of the others in an idealised
Soup Kitchen they may do so. Thousands
in life as it is here do this continually iu one
way or another—do it kindly and gladly,
according to their ability. But the weak-
lings, the afflicted, whether with natural
disability or dog-laziness, do not swagger
round as their equals. They do not demand
halves of everything as a right and aspire to
the bossing of things. If they did, the milk
of human kindness would quickly dry up in
the rest. The monotony of it all would soon
become tiresome.
# • x #
Under the most favoured conditions, and
with a community of persons absolutely sin-
cere, unselfish, and unambitious, the experi-
ment might be a partial success. It might
succeed as similar organisations, held to-
gether by religious vows, have succeeded in
the past —and that is nob to any great extent.
Such communities had a strong, selfish
incentive to unselfishness, which the New
Australians have not. Just as the Hindoo
devotee propitiates his idols by enduring
infernal tortures ; just as Christian martyrs
faced death for eternal life, they were
dominated by the most intense, and often
the meanest, of selfish human desires—that
of saving their own souls. The New Austra-
lians look chiefly or entirely to the best
means of compassing their material welfare.
Each of them wants to do the best he can for
himself, and professes to think this can be
accomplished by dividing up what he pro-
duces with all the rest. Some do think it,
no doubt. Some don’t think at all, probably,
bub are carried away by their feelings—
“they are dangerous guides, the feelings.”
And a few at least may be credited with
knowing better, and calculating upon per-
sonally keeping on top while the new Utopia
lasts. x
# » •
The individual who wants to keep on top
wo have always with us. The problem of
“ Who's to do the washing-up ?” is one which
nine out of ten human beings will always
seek to solve to his or her own satisfaction
by shunting that unlovely job on someone
else. In New Australia, should other frosty
circumstances not nip the bud of the pro-
ject, this influence will ultimately bring dis-
ruption and chaos. Yery few of the members
will be content to go on according to a
popular vote doing more work than others
for the same pay, or fulfilling tasks which
are specially distasteful. There will be the
inevitable schemings, and heart-burnings,
and bitternesses, the inevitable -struggle for
front seats. To suppose there will not is to
suppose that Lane’s followers, “drawn from
country districts ’ though they may be, are
different from all other people who have
lived. They may think they are for the
time being, as so many under stress of
emotional exaltation have done before, only
to find afterwards the bitterness of their
mistake and reap its disasters. Like the
Kawealians, who were mostly selected men
and women, sincere, intelligent, enthusiastic,
aud who began an enterprise of the same
kind under much fairer conditions, the New
Australians will assuredly quarrel, even to
dissolution over the great questions of dis-
tributing the loaves and fishes, and washing
up.
• # •
In their case, the loaves are more than
doubtful, but the extract we give below
points to the possibility of fish diet as a
dernier ressort. It is taken verbatim from the
Buenos Ayres Standard, the leading daily
paper of South America, of March 12 last -
less than three months ago, and since 'the
New Australia explorers returned :
In La Drmocracia of 2nd March
is signed by some W Freiidiuien, »ho mje ui Qoverll .
plaints of the way they have been treated y
" They proceed to state that they were induced to come
to this colony attracted by a-little book.
of the President Gonzales Colon.}. a ud that
promises contained therein are P ia< ?tiCc made Uoe of
a Government without self-respect has y colonists say
them for the purposes of speculation. The
That they liave overcome many ““ the" fields
as a proof of their industry and bald work, tne
which were but a short time ago virgin forest They ha
800 acres-under cultivation, and there would have be
more had the Government not violated its promises,
the result that the colonists have been p unged in)
most dreadful misery. They complin that they
entitled to receive provisions and implemer
price, whereas they are now obliged to go to Pm ate
stores, where they are mercilessly fleeced
promised a provisional title so soon as th y - i
lots; still, after a year’s waiting, “ not one has received a
title,”' amlthe questisius-asked ;Is not .all this < done V
serve some occult intent of the Minister of C •
According to the regulations, each lot, as soon as the
colonist begins to work it, becomes his property, conqi
tionally, and he may dispose of all it con ans but
Minister Lopez and M. Jacquet prohibit him from dis
posing of the timber felled on the ot, the Government
reserving the right of disposing of it to whomei er they
please, on whatever terms they like. . v
1 They were promised for the first six months a subsid}
of 50 cents per dav for each adult, and 20 cents for each
child ; but only a part of the colonists have received this
•during the whole of that period. Bullocks also were
promised to those having 8 acres of land ready for the
plough: but many of them with 16 acres ready have been
refused them. . , , , . ~
On the occasion of the Minister s last visit to the
colony he, in an eloquent discourse, recommended the
colonists to go on working, and that the Government worn
do all they could for them. Three days afterwards the
subsidy was stopped, which obliged them, so as to avoid
starvation, to sell their standing crops at a low price to
the shopkeepers and other speculators who, under tne
protection of Government, fell upon them like a rapacious
horde. Furthermore, when the Commissary of the co-
lony found that they had applied to the French Govern-
ment for the means of getting out of the hell into which
their credulity had brought them, he ordered a notice to
be put up to the effect that any colonist who should
attempt to leave would be arrested as a common male-
factor. The abuses committed would fill a volume, and it
is only for the public to form an opinion as to what kind
of administration it must be when, after spending 100,000
dollars, the colonists are naked and starving, and are
obliged to seek in fishing and such like for the means
of existence. This is the way the Minister of Colonisation
foments immigration, to the glory and profit of the
country.
# # *
In the faitli that the electric spark of
something or another thrills all their bosoms
and will inspire the strong to go on bearing
the burdens of the weak, and obeying the
orders of the foolish or cunning, the New
Australia scheme has been conceived. In
the more insane faith still that the Para-
guayan Government will stand by pro-
mises which, if they were reliable, and the
place were worth living in at all, would have
caused every acre of the country to be
settled long ago, the expedition is going out.
The end of it, probably, will not be so
disastrous as that of many other South
American dreams. Most of the voyagers,
after a troubled epoch of the swamp fevers,
and yerha mate , should be able to come back
to Old Australia enlightened by a satisfying
experience.
The Imperial Institute Opening.
Several circumstances attendant upon the
dedication of the great new temple of Jingo,
whose uprearing has been inspired and directed
by primrose-eating-Tories, idolatrous slaverers
of Royalty, masters and servants of Caste, and
all manhoodless jackalls, courtiers, and tuft-
hunters of Imperialism, were remarkable. For
the matter of that, the whole of the official
proceedings were remarkable, not so much in
the sense of a national event, as in that of a
national exposure. The classes who wish to
maintain the integrity of the Empire as a
whole, and clasp its growing and restive out-
posts in the bonds of a brotherhood for war and
taxation in their behalf, felt it needful to make
a supreme and garish effort. So the courts, in
which tributes ol loyalism from snobbish misre-
presentatives of British colonies and possessions
were heaped, had to be opened with what a
civilised press calls “impressive ceremonies.”
The Colonists and the Possessed had to be won
over finally, and every sentiment of independ-
ence and republicanism subjugated in their
bosoms by an unparalleled display of the King-
dom and the Power and the Glory.
* * #
Possibly it might all have seemed impressive
to “the poor Indian, whose untutored mind
clothes him in front and leaves him bare
behind.” The Kanaka, who forfeits his placid
island life (when he doesn’t happen to be kid-
napped) to sweat at “trashing” sugarcane
under the North Queensland sun for love of the
red bandana of Manchester and the twelve-
bladed pocket-knife of Birmingham, might
have been visibly affected by the griev-
ous and blatant paganisms of the occa-
sion. The proceedings seem to have par-
taken of the characteristics of a Lord Mayor’s
show and a Somali love-feast. They revealed
the abiding British faith in barbaric ostenta-
tion, and the aldermanic craving for shine of
pinch beck and sound of brass trumpets. A blaring
proclamation of the doctrine of British Respec-
tability, a national outburst of Cheap Jackery,
meant to allure fools from the Colonies and the
Possessions into selling their substantialities
for meretricious gewgaws and tinselled baubles,
that is what the opening of the Imperial
Institute amounted to. A tawdry, barbaric
bazaar, where cheap, wholesale buyers sought to
meet cheap sellers, and the commodities dealt
in were the earnings, the liberties, and the
lives of peoples of the present and future.
* * *
The whole strength of the company, so to
speak, gathered in the usual Richardsonian
fashion on the platform outside of the tent, in
full stage costume. The street procession was
a glittering, interminable string of Royalties,
sub-Royalties, andpicturesquely-tailored nobili-
ties, bandits and spongers on the public, such as
has seldom been seen even in England. Belted
Earls by the score, whose forbears haven’t done
a useful tap of w»ork for generations, and who
wouldn’t think of associating, as a general
thing, with anybody who had. Dukes who
have each, in each year of their lives, consumed
the earnings of hundreds of miserable families
at home, and who want to go on doing it
abroad, backed up by the power of an in-
divided Army and Navy, obedient to the rule
Things As They Are and Ever Shall Be, World
without End, if the Empire can only be ken't
together and run upon its present lines.
Queen set a peal of bells, presented by a Mel-
bourne female, going, by means of a key made
of Victorian gold and Broken Hill silver." Then
Apothecary L. L. Smith, made opulent and great
by the pound-notes of young men who didn’t
feel well, “ crooked the pregnant hinges of U le
knee ” in obeisance before .the Prince of Bac-
carat-gamblers, with elaborately wrought cata-
logues of Victorian offerings at the shrine o|
Grovel. Infinite littleness seizing a large hoard
ing to advertise itself upon ; oppressive privilege
with the death-rattle in its throat, strutting
abroad in brave masquerade, and trusting t„
the,, old stage-gags, with the doom of Time and
Knowledge written on its forehead ! The unity
of the Empire, the greatness of the undivided
British people, and the necessity of cohesion in
the interests of them all and of freedom— that
was the patter.
* * •
Eew Australians would have been taken in
by it, anyhow. But, if some were likely to
believe that this flaring Imperialistic corrob-
boree was prompted by true patriotic desire
for the general well-being of British descendants
everywhere, one circumstance which occurred
should save them from making a bad mistake,
At the reception given by the Prince of Wales,
after the opening rites, the assemblage cheered
Lord Salisbury and hooted Mr. Gladstone.
That simple fact gives the whole business away,
The New York Times truthfully described it
as “ a display of highly-developed snobbery and
a survival of savagery impossible in America,”
* # «
It’s more than that. Only deep-seated
original blackguardism could thus heap insult
on "the grey head of a man who, at the edge of
the grave, is engaged in the greatest political
battle that has been fought in England during
this century. From the participants in anil
figure-heads of such vulgar and meaningless
ceremonies, not much in the way of sentimental
decency could be looked for. Conduct, that in
the United States, under Popular Government,
would be regarded as evidence of insanity, may
be allowed to pass among the titled descendants
of marauders and murderers. The gross act
itself need not surprise us, but the plain
motive of it should enlighten such Australians
as need enlightenment.
* # #
Mr. Gladstone stands for the extension of
popular liberties. He is the leader of that
major section of the British people which thinks
that the power of government should he vested
in those who arg governed, as a whole, instead
of being entrusted to a powerful privileged
minority. The political purpose which inspires
him is that of securing for the people of Ireland
the form of Parliamentary representation
which a great majority of them has desired for
years past. He does not ask that more should
be given them than has been given to every
one of the Australian provinces. The justice of
the claim made has gradually impressed the
people, not only of Great Britain, but of the
civilised world. The insistence by the
Tory lords and Imperialistic masters that
a so - called Union, brought about by
treachery and held together with blood, shall
stand as it now stands, lias become a burning
disgrace to the Imperial Parliament. The rela-
tion of Ireland to the Imperial Government has, .
ever since the Union, been that of a vassal Stale.
Its soil is chiefly owned by titled and other
Conservative personages who live abroad in
safety upon the substance of which they rob
the people under sanction of British laws ami
under protection of British military forces.
r # # *
It is because Mr. Gladstone heads the army
of peace and true union, which constitutionally
seeks to give the Irish people a right to manage
their own public affairs, that he was hootee.
For this, the Indian princes, gouty landlords,
aldermanic parasites, and C.M.G.’s opene
their mouths to hiss at him. And because
Lord Salisbury tries to block the path of Home
Rule, and to drag Ireland for ever behind i
Imperial chariot-wheels, he was applaudei J
the same crowd.
# * *
It is not the fact that Ireland has been scan-
dalously misgoverned, and her people mai
miserable and rebellious, that has enlis ec
her side the sympathy of the masses,
the conviction, everywhere growing, ■
people can govern themselves muci
satisfactorily than other people cau £°. ■
them, and that they have a perfect clam
to do so if they like. It w n° '
faction of the Irish people fed on foreign g >
as Lord Salisbury says, who have J orce <
Home Rule question into the central arena
practical politics. Irish demands b aV ®
brought within sight of fulfilment, .
they are felt to be just, with a streng 1
ing that cannot be withstood. Mw l .
“ foreign gold ” has been contribute I
tralians, who are quite contented 0 L

speaks in the Imperial interest, a se
take good care to remain “foreign m
to the end. Eight-tenths of the ra 10 j or
and women of Australia favour Home
Ireland, not because they have special j r
tines with that country, but because J
Heme Rule themselves, and mean to Keep
*** I 1
That Australian snobs and lickspittles
give their “ foreign gold” to help, 111 *jL),
Institute is deplorable, but it can , p s

V Tf. debts ”by £BOO,OOO, of which amount
Tt least £600,000 consisted of advances to, or
n the shares of, limited liability companies
ri ch complntei the said Matthew Henry
Datles was, in every case, one of the promoters
and of which companies he was, in the majority of
cases the chairman of directors, and in which
said companies he had, in every case, a pre-

attached to his information a memo, alleging
that, at the previous half-yearly balancing, the
“cash at bankers’” had been swelled by the
inclusion of bogus cheques to the amount of
£66 400, these paper-moneys being paid into
the Mercantile on August 1, ’9l, by persons or
companies directly connected with Davies, and
withdrawn by other cheques two or three days
later. It is only the alleged facts advanced
bv Simpson and others that the grand jury
will be called upon to consider. No opinion
as to the guilt or innocence of Chairman
Davies and Manager Millidge is expected from
the grand jury. It has merely to say whether
the allegations constitute a prima facie case
against the accused persons, or otherwise.
Meanwhile, Victoria in general, and Melbourne
most particularly, is full of misgivings as to the
settlement of even this simple point. The
special jurors’ list contains the names of scores
of influential bank-overdrafters and active
enemies to “disclosures,” who are doing all
that lies in their very considerable power to
stop the Mercantile case from coming to an
issue. Of its history thus far, an ex-Minister
of the Crown (Peacock, M.L.A.) says : “ I am
astounded at the underhand influences which
have been at work. I am grieved to find pro-
minent men in the community log-rolling in
the basest fashion. The best interests of the
country have been subordinated to suit the
tactics of a small handful of men. I say these
things deliberately, because 1 am possessed of
information which should impel any person of
common honesty to speak out. ... lam
positively staggered at the revelations made to
me by responsible people.” Evidently, then, this
is no time for believing that a Melbourne grand
jury doesn’t require to be carefully scrutinised
by the community whose Law is said to be
dominated by a handful of corruptionists. It
has not escaped notice that the counsel who
obtained an order for a grand jury from the
Pull Court threw out the suggestion that 23
jurors should be secured. 23 is the full number
allowed, but it would seem that there is no
legal guarantee that the attendance of empan-
nelled persons shall go higher than 12. Not
less than 12 jurors can agree to find a true bill,
therefore, one “ cronk ” Melbournian amongst
the minimum grand jury would bring about a
miscarriage of justice which 11 “ cronk ” ditto
could not accomplish on a tribunal of the maxi-
mum strength. Presuming that a grand jury
over-rules the decision of the Attorney-General
and finds a true bill against Davies and
iiLLiDGE, and presuming that the same
Attorney-General directs the Crown Law
epartment to resume the prosecution which,
o le general dissatisfaction of Australia, was
eoently dropped, a sceptical country will pos-
y demand a reconstruction of the Crown’s
gal policy. “Up to now,” says Peacock,
C" 4 ;’ “every point and every feature has
strained in favour of the defendants.”

At the humiliation and prayer demonstration in
Melbourne Town Hall, after Bishop Goe had
delivered an address, the congregation sang,
“ God moves in a mysterious way.” This shows
that even now the church hasn’t begun to grasp
the situation. It was the fact that Matthew
Davies and Bent and Munro moved in such a
mysterious way that was the main cause of the
trouble. The insinuation that the Deity went
out of its way to move in a more mysterious
fashion than usual for the express purpose of
making suburban allotments go down to their
natural value, is only another example of the
diseased self-importance of the average cleric,
who, if he chances to stub his toe against a log
with a nail in it, or if he over-eats himself and
gets a pain in the dinner-tank, always says that
he is being “ chastened ”by Heaven. Also, in
a certain infiatedly bumble fashion, he is proud
of being “chastened,” just as the ordinary
carnal groveller is proud of having been kicked
by a prince. If anybody could convince the
church that it wasn’t being specially chastened
it would be the roughest thing that has hap-
pened to it since the Reformation.
Dear Bulletin, —Are you not rather rough on
the “cockatoo”? Here in S.A. his principal
reason for being Conservative is that he owns
his freehold—or has a right of purchase—and
so listens to the National Ass re confiscation,
&c. Where they are leaseholders —and over
3,300,000 acres are let on perpetual lease to
farmers —they, for the most part, support the
Labour-party in the proposal for an increase of
land-tax and a decrease in Customs duties. It
is true the average “ cockie ” wants everybody
to be made to work 16 hours a day and that he
is deplorably mean, but his own wretched life
is the cause of it. And his life is more wretched
than ever now with wheat at half a-crown a
bushel, and horse-flesh so cheap that it doesn’t
pay to breed it. Look at his own wizened
face, his dried-up, careworn wife, his hulking
sons and clumsy daughters. Not one of them
has any joy in life ; the highest ideal of the
women is the Methodist chapel, of the men the
Saturday night in the pub. And those who
know the Australian farmer know that neither
talking or writing will make him any* better.
Nothing but example can do it, and unless the
State sets the example I don’t know who will.
Apart from low prices, most of the misery of
agricultural life is due to the solitary farm
system. If we had farm villages there
would be society and companionship, and
it is society that has made civilisation.
There would be a library, a public garden,
and an assembly-room for meetings and
dances. If the Anglo-Saxon could so far forget
himself as to “ bunch ” the cooking for a dozen
families the impossible would have been achieved
and the slavery of the farmer’s wife and
daughters would end. They would have time
to read, to cultivate flowers, and to visit. The
sons would no longer be louts thirsting to get
to the city, the daughters no longer gawky
gigglers. This change is possible right off, if
anything short of dynamite could move the
bucolic and make him gregarious, as he is in
other countries. Under the altered circum-
stances it might even be found possible to give
the farm hand something better than the reap-
ing machine to camp in during winter.— An
Ex-Cockie.
*
Don W. Lane, the boss argonaut of Paraguay,
writes mystical letters to the daily press at
intervals about the glories of “ New Australia,”
and his outpourings are reminiscent partly of
the Mahdi prophesying in the desert with foam
dropping on his beard, and partly of Mrs.
Hominy, the bony apostle of the unutterable
and the yellow, skinny dreamer about the
development of matter. Every now and then
some cold, realistic doubter expresses a sus-
picion that Paraguay, after all, may be very
little better than our Northern Territory, and
suggests that the Government is unstable, and
markets are remote, and titles shaky, and
things generally unpromising. Then the founder
and principal dreamer of the expedition rises up
and replies with a hunk of eloquence about the
destiny of man, and the hidden forces that are
driving him onward, so that he is mysteriously
shoved behind by the great inevitable, whereby
he must ultimately concuss against the unseen,
and become absorbed in space. For example :
Life must go on. We cannot stop it if we would ; we
cannot oppose to the supreme law that inspires it our
puny preventitives or our timid fears. Behind life is a
force that laughs at death, that mocks at disease, that
ignores pain. "Whence” and “whither” we may dis-
pute over, but on the solemn fact of living there is no
dispute, not even in the metaphysical thought which sees
in existence but the dreaming of a dream. And whether
we come again into life, or whether it is only the little
ones who come and go like bubbles on a brook, life is and
is always-and we have part in its making. It is the
future life that judges the past life, inexorably, inevit-
ably. And when our present is past, what shall we say
if we have sown evil and given no thought to the good—
if we have gone out into the darkness with our selfishness
encrusting us as a shroud, and our souls scorched with the
burning frost of indifference and inhumanity ?
The above is a comforting thing to the man who
has risked his all in the Paraguayan madness.
The Bulletin can see a vision of the New
Australian settlement as it will be some day in
the future. There will be a few hundred
people digging and fencing in a dreary, hopeless
fashion out in the great loneliness, and living
on woe and unsaleable vegetables and dreams
of home. And, meanwhile, the founded of the
settlement will be foaming at the mouth and
uttering poetry beneath a tree, and wildly ask-
ing the damp ferns, “ What is life ? ” There is
altogether too much poetry and too little re-
liable market for potatoes about “ New Aus-
tralia.”
*
Mr. John Howell, manager of the Proprietary
mine, and mining-companies’ representative on
the New South Wales Lead-poisoning Commis-
sion, declined to sign the report of that extra-
parliamentary body. Which is, perhaps, the
best guarantee that could be given that the
report is worth its cost. Summarised, the
report finds that lead-poisoning is not an un-
avoidable accompaniment of the Broken Hill
situation, but is more or less preventible, and
certain recommendations are made in respect
of precautionary safeguards. As far as the
Dices Government is concerned, the obtaining
of this report has probably fulfilled the whole
idea of its duty. It can point to the Commis-
sion and to its little bill, and it can shelve all
further action pending reconsideration. As the
mine-owners’ man wouldn’t sign the report,
and there being no compulsory law respecting
its recommendations, the mine-owners may be
relied upon to do no better straight away. The
New South Wales Labour members might for
once make a united move on behalf of their
working mates.
♦
One of the chief features of the Queensland
elections was the defeat of Isidor Lissner,
Minister for Mines, by the Ravenswood miners.
All that money and influence could do to secure
his return was done in the most shameless
manner—storekeepers stopping miners’ credit,
and even threatening to take back explosives in
use on some of the mines. Vehicles and beers
were plentiful on the Lissner side, while Jack-
son, the Labour candidate, had neither. The
contest was complicated by the presence of a
third candidate, a local solicitor, who bid for
the labour-vote; yet so thoroughly did the
miners stick together that the solicitor did not
get as many votes as there were members on
his committee. The little Minister, at his last
meeting on the eve of the polling, did not
invite questions, but an elector obtained leave
to put two. These were asked and answered
as follows :
“Is it not a fact that, under the Land-grant Railway Act,
a miner cannot begin to win gold or silver until he has
sunk 70 feet below the surface, and then has to pay for
surface damages tor shafts or approaches?” The Minister
replied: “That is so." “That being so,” said the ques-
tioner, “is it not consummate boldness—not to say
audacity—on your part, as Minister for Mines, the
Minister who is supposed to safeguard the interests of the
mining community all over the province, to come to a
mining constituency and ask the miners to return you in
order to assist to carry out this nefarious job ? ”
The Minister did not reply, and the audience
shrieked with derisive laughter.
They’ll Do!
Those who had the privilege of breakfasting with the
Governor and lady were very much impressed with the
affable and easy manner of both. Lady Duff was de-
scribed by one gentleman as “an improved edition of
Lady Carington,” whose gentleness and vivacity Sydney
people so well remember. Sir Robert created a very good
impression by his genial, manly demeanor; and the
opinion has already been expressed that the new Governor
and his wife will soon become warm favourites among the
Australians.— Evening News.
We feared the very worst —we thought
They really mightn’t have been able
To act as decent Christians ought
When seated at a breakfast table ;
Thank God ! our doubts are set at rest
The Bulletin once more breathes freely—
Thank God, they’ve passed the crucial test,
And acted, on the whole, genteely !
Our new-arrived vice-regal pair
Will do this “colony ” much credit;
Those who were privileged to share
Their morning meat have boldly said it.
Watched closely by the anxious eyes
Of “ gentlemen ” who mark such matters,
They lapsed into no gaucheries,
Nor swallowed knives, nor licked their platters !
They didn’t eat like people who
Are Uas6 and of grub disdainful.
Nor rush the business and chew
The steaks and chops with noises painful;
They didn’t sneeze, or snort, or cough,
Or make the cloth all round them greasy ;
We’re glad to know their manner of
Negotiating food was “ easy.”
The Governor was self-possessed
And firm ; his mode of mastication
Showed hidden force which may attest
Itself upon some great occasion;
His elbows overset no cup,
No wayward crumb stuck in his-neck fast
And neither would go down or up—
A calm dictator to his breakfast.
He wore an etiquette-book smile
That Darling Point could not find fault with.
And showed no wild confusion while
He took his egg a pinch of salt with ;
He talked in unembarrassed way,
Of topics of the Sunny South full;
Said just the thing he tried to say,
And never said it with his mouth full.
We feared Sir Robert might have “ stacked ”
His plate and, like a wolf or vulture,
All victuals within reach attacked,
But Appetite allied with Culture
Distinguished our distinguished guest,
Whose style was happy, free, and genial,
Who no cold, surly thoughts expressed,
Nor cursed at the attendant menial!
His wife—we mean his Lady—too,
Was to close scrutiny subjected,
Which she appears to have come through
With credit great and unexpected.
“ Dear Lady Carington ” was “ sweet,”
So they declared who could effuse so;
Now she must go to a back seat—
A “gentleman ” has told the News so !
She was “ vivacious ” in her day
Of social headship and position,
And now—well, she has gone away
And we have “ an improved edition ” 1
They’ll do, in short. The Governor
A gentleman is, bona-fide
(A thing we have some openings for),
His wife, they say’s, a puffick lydy !
Someone threw a brick the other day at
the train in which Gladstone was travelling, and
broke a window alongside him. The cable re-
marks ; “ It is believed that the stone was thrown
by some person politically opposed to Mr. Glad-
stone, with the object of personally injuring him.”
This looks probable. Still it is just possible that
the boulder was thrown to amuse Gladstone.

(1) Ireland returns 103 members to the Commons; on
Nov. 14, ’92, these consisted of—Bo Home Rulers (71 anti-
Parnellites and 9 Parnellites) and 23 anti-Home Rulers
(19 Tories and 4 Liberal Unionists). Besides these, there
was one anti-Parnellite Home Ruler for an English pro-
vincial borough, making the total Home Rule strength
81 ; Gladstonian Liberals, conceding a measure of Home
Rule, 273. Total for Home Rule Bill, 354. Total against,
310 (Tories, 269 ; Liberal Unionists, 47). (2) It is impos-
sible to state “about the number of the Irish people who
want Home Rule,” but here are some figures we have
sorted out. In the general election, ’92, there were in Ire-
land 303,301 votes cast for, and 83,930 votes against Home
Rule ; there were Home Rul ts also returned unopposed
for districts having 61,711 voters on their rolls ; and anti-
Home Rulers returned unopposed for districts having
101,050 voters on their rolls. Putting these figures
together (though most rolls are a good deal bogus), you
get as a give-and-take result, Irish Home Rule voters if.
evidence, 365,012; Dish Unionist voters, 184,980,

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
7th June, 1893.
My Dear Moorabinda—
Lady Duff begins very cannily. She says she
cannot remember names, but she “never forgets a
face.” The old, old trick of the suave society
person who doesn’t know who on earth these
people are, but receives them all the same with a
warmth of manner which seems to imply that Miss
Dash’s freckles are seared into her very soul,
although Miss Dash’s name quite escapes her for
the moment. Lady Duff has come to a bonny
place for exercising her non-face-forgetting faculty;
but I rather think she will lose confidence in her-
self after her first general reception—or, I mean, I
hope she will. Any leading lady at Government
House who couldn’t forget at least some of the
visages I have seen steering a changeless course
for the sandwiches on mob-days ought to have
prayers offered up for her in all the churches.
I hear that Gov. Duff’s first levee at Govern-
ment House the other Monday did not pass off
without one or two hitches—incidental to all first
performances, by the way. One thing was that a
number of dignitaries, who ought to have been
posed in the red drawing-room by virtue of
private entree, were whirled about in the crowd
of ordinary folks in the vestibule. I don’t know,
though, how anybody could have mistaken the
aide-de-camp for the Governor, but it seems some
really did. Men would plunge into the room and
grasp the a.d.c. blindly by the hand and pursue
their mad career, only to be directed back to the
Governor, whose bright smile—so fatally near a
laugh when this sort of thing was happening—is
probably haunting some of them still.
Mr. Galton, private sec. to Sir Robert Duff,
wears a tiny stripe of tartan on the near
side of his coat on occasions of state. It isn’t
effective —it only gives the Kyrle-Bellewish secre-
tary the appearance of the youth who knots blue
ribbon about his person to reassure an anxious
world that he never goes beyond ginger-pop. And,
apropos of the Gov.’s suite, I hear on lovely
authority that what both of them remarked about
Australian belledom was its “ stern and steadfast
withering glare fixed on our faltering features
there.” Verb, sap! But I have over and over
again noticed that even the most ordinarily dis-
creet maiden will coolly inspect a new man who
happens either to be a spectacle or closely con-
nected with one, as if he was nothing more
emotional than a tailor’s-dummy.
Sydney Tattersall’s Club held a rather melan-
choly race-meeting last Saturday, but still it may
live in history as being Governor Duff’s very first
appearance at Randwick. His Ex. turned up in a
sporting-looking fawn covert coat and a dark
necktie—to the manifest despair of those youths
who had almost strangled themselves with shriek-
ing plaid scarfs in the hope that the new person-
age would sport the family token on his haber-
dashery. And just here it occurs to me that I
don’t believe the Governor has a tartan. lam
told that it is only the head of a clan who is per-
mitted to dress his followers in chromatic spasms,
and I think that, after all the mental strain, it may
be discovered by some rigid authority that there is
no Duff clan, and that our Duff is only a
Saxon and merely privileged to dress himself
with shepherd’s plaid. By the way, I think
Governor D. ought always to give, not take the
odds on a racecourse, don’t you ? The luck of
“ laying on ” may run in the family.
The Austrian Archduke Ferdinand and his
cousin tried to shear a sheep with the Wolseley
machine in Sydney the other day. The Arch-
duke’s attempt was rather hopeful, but his cousin,
who wore a pair of large gold bangles, was not so
successful. His bangles tripped him up at critical
moments, although he shook them back with the
impatient grace of a young lady whose bracelets
come over her fingers when she’s pawing the
piano. Somebody ought to have explained that
the Australian shearer scarcely ever wears bangles
when at work.
Mrs. Mayoress Manning’s second reception (on
last Monday), in her rooms at Sydney Town Hall,
was a pleasant afternoon function. Mrs. Manning
wore a good-looking dress of steel-grey satin
brocade, elaborately trimmed with black velvet
and jet—small bonnet, en suite. Some unwise
admirer had given her a dozen or so buttonholers
of violets to hold. They “ went ” with the dress
very well, but as they weren’t tied together they
seemed rather a slippery handful for the popular
hostess. Miss Manning, who made a übiquitous
sub-hostess, wore a severely cut gown of dark-
blue, topped by a black hat and feathers —but the
afternoon was so cloudy you wanted a bull’s-eye
lantern indoors to see just what the crowd was
wearing. When the electric-light was turned on,
things became much better—especially for the
woman with glittering jet concealed about her
person, who began to twinkle, twinkle, like a
little star. There was a very smart crowd—but
before I stop I must congratulate Mrs. Manning
on introducing an afternoon-tea-cup that you can
get your nose into. It’s quite time the up-ended
thimble of the festive tea-tray were passed on, more
in sorrow than in anger, to the nursery doll’s-
house.
The most human touch in “Human Nature”
Sydney Lyceum, is that the wicked young

In “ The Idler,” at Sydney Criterion, it strikes
me as being unwise for Lady Harding (Mrs.
Brough) to wear such a very becoming wrap of
black velvet, trimmed with mongolian fur, and
flapping back to show pink linings; also, to be
so careful about her hair when she goes to argue
with her bold, bad lover. I can’t help thinking
that if she had diplomatically turned-up with her
hair in curl-papers, an old flannelette wrapper
with a button or two at large, carpet slippers
worn-out, and a faint suggestion of fried fish,
the naughty man would have let her go at sight,
and ventilated his room afterwards. In the banquet
scene, Mrs. Brough wore a well-fitting cream-
satin, aggravated by diamonds. Miss Temple’s
best dress was a cinnamon-brown, made with a bell
skirt and full sleeves, the sleeveless bodice of black
silk fitting her like a glove. I thought Mr. Bouci-
cault as Simeon Strong looked very like Bellew’s
Scarpia when he sat writing at the table ; and, by-
the-way, is it the author’s fault that Titheradge
upsets the doddering grammar of the d.c. by
ejaculating—“Me who deserve it so little ”?
Tempo Refuge Ball, which has engulphed th e
Fresh Air League Ball as a whale swallows a
tittlebat, is to come off at Sydney Town Hall to-
night (Thursday). Sir Robert and Lady Duff are
to be present, and no two ladies will be allowed to
dance together.
I saw a really uncommon frock in “front” on
the first night of “Human Nature.” Imagine
black tulle thickly embroidered in heavy gold
spiders, the low bodice of black tulle and gold
filagree cut in wings on the shoulders —but the
best of it was you couldn’t see that it was cut at
all—it seemed to happen like the sheath of a lily.
If anybody had worn it on the stage what a dive
there would have been for opera glasses ! And I
thought that the worst thing about her dress
was that it reminded you of the sort of paralyser
a society actress plays “ Camille ” in.
On dit that the most astonishing thing about the
recent marriages (down South) of a couple of
fashionable barmaids was that the doctor married
the lady whose billowy figure was mostly gutta-
percha ! Medical science (surgeons are all very
well at a post-mortem) has still a lot to learn
evidently.
The N.S.W. Silkgrowers Association (still in
the chrysalis) is quite the latest fad emanating
from the Ladies’ Literary Club. It is responsible
for much enthusiasm, as if the world were only
waiting for a cream-coloured moth to lay eggs on
a mulberry-leaf and settle the question, “ What
shall we do with our girls?” But, whether the
fascinations of the silk-worm are strong enough to
lure femininity away from shop-windows to pick
leaves off mulberry-bushes and watch the inter-
esting grub how he gorges, will only be learnt by
experience (shares one-pound each).
The splendour of the plush-clad cook at 255.
weekly seems temporarily diminished in Sydney,
and the old percentage of one Mary Ann to’six
missuses must be read the other way up. A
Sydney lady who recently went in search of a
housemaid, found that the crowd of applicants
extended into the street —and another woman who
wanted a “general,” arrived on the whole registry
office in tears ! Odd to think that the moment is
at hand when an Australian house-mother can
actually strike an assured attitude and dictate :
“Bridget, I allow no followers, and your holiday
is the afternoon of the fourth Sunday in the
month-only.” A great sign of the subdued state
of the labour-market is that man has taken to
wearing shirts again—at least, the pleading “no
shirts ” advertisements dwindle visibly every day.
What a side-light it is on feminine inquisitive-
ness that the drapers who run bargain sales in-
variably whitewash their windows and confidently
await the excited mob ? When a woman can
flatten her little nose against the panes and drink
her fill of bargains from the neutral ground of the
public pavement she hasn’t the same incentive to
rush in and rescue the enterprising tradesman
from the jaws of the silk moth as she has when a
sheet of pipe-clay intervenes between her and the
“ sacrifices ” which then become golden mysteries
to her piqued curiosity. lam certain that if
married men only knew how much a white-washed
window costs them there would be a deputation of
aggrieved husbands to the Mayor in less than no
time. And in this connection it occurs to me that
a city must be very insolvent indeed when even
the shop-windows want whitewashing.
The clerks in several Sydney banks have had
their lunch-time altered to a later hour than usual
because the “directors wish to prevent their
officers loitering round the street-preachers !” The
banks have evidently had quite enough Sunday-
school superintendent, thank you ; but this altera-
tion (the young man with the tartan tie and tan
boots calls it “arbitrary”) will make a regular
revolution in Block time. Young ladies who
intercept the hungry bank-clerk on his way to his
noontide bun will please accept this intimation.
I think that “Faustine,” in Sydney Daily
Telegraph, puts her objection to flowers at
funerals on an erroneous basis. It is surely not
the “ waste ” involved in the tribute that consti-
tutes the ground of indictment; you might as well
talk of the waste of shoe-leather in visiting the
grave, or the waste of zinc-lining in a crematorial
urn. The objection to flowers as at present
utilised in funeral purposes is that they so often
are an advt of a bathetic posturer in the first
place, and of a sharp florist in the second, while
not rarely they are the mere try-on of a tricky
tradesman who intends to force the bill on to the
victim “when the funeral’s done.” “ A flower,”
as Heine says, “friend, if you love me, spare me
a flower.” Heine would not call the flower given
by a friend “a waste;” but the flowers the
dead get in these days are mostly given by cheap-
johns and mountebanks.
I am told that at a very swagger dinner at
Toorak, a well-known society girl wore her long
gloves all through the meal. “Why do you wear
your gloves ?” asked the Johnnie who sat by her.
“Because my arms are so thin,” was the reply,
“ but mamma says I’m very well made everywhere
else.”
Yours faithfully.

Alexandra Variety Show.
Haddon Chambers’ drama, “The Idler,
was presented on Saturday at the Criterion, this
bein''- its second appearance in Sydney. Un tne
other occasion, it eventuated under various adverse
conditions at the Garrick, but in the present
instance it gets as good an opportunity as the
hungriest drama need ask for, and the result is
mostly satisfactory. The only possible objection
that can be taken to it is that the central incident
seems to have been kept rather long, and
possibly a little high in consequence. It isn t
altogether a new-laid incident —in fact, it seems to
be partly hatched. But “ The Idler is
full of strong situations, and is a play ot
infinite possibilities. There is no oppressive
villain in it, and no hero whose conspicuous good-
ness weighs like a Himalaya on the soul.
There isn’t too much of it; the writer
knew where to stop, and he doesn’t wait
to tell whether the two marriages come off or
not, or whether the other man ever gets back
from the North Pole or is lost among the frozen
seas and bears. The language put in Titheradge s
mouth is the perfection of gentlemanly seduction ;
and when the lady calls on him secretly and at
night, apparently for the express purpose of being
lost, the situation is so well managed that no one
seems to realise how many serious possibilities
there are in it, although it almost seems as if she
was going to be lost in full view of the audience.
It is the old patriarchal story of the Fall over
again, only the parties fall politely in evening
dress, or rather one of them tries hard to
fall but the other won’t. In the old days
of Genesis, man fell in an uncouth fashion
in abbreviated bathing-nostume, but times have
changed since then. “The Idler” is a genuine
triumph for Mrs. Brough, who plays a difficult
part with pathos and dignity ; and for Boucicault,
whose American millionaire is all that a million-
aire ought to be. This latter remark is based on
the writer’s acquaintance with one millionaire,
who was distinctly everything that such a person
ought not to be—in fact, he was everything that
everybody ought not to be except a hangman or a
gorilla. Titheradge, as the Idler, is an attenuated
success, and it is noticeable that certain objec-
tionable mannerisms are rapidly growing on
Titheradge. Theworstis a curious habit of shutting
his eyes most of the time, which is suggestive of a
blind man what has happened to his
dog, and the next worst is a custom of chewing
the air as if he were dining on space. Misses
Homer and Temple, Mr. Brough, as an ancient
gentleman in goggles who asks Titheradge for his
mother’s hand, and Cecil Ward, are all satis-
factory. So, also, is Miss Nelly Mortyne, a lady
whom the audience would gladly see oftener, and
who has a great deal to recommend her as a
gushing widow in a frock that is cut low at the
front. Wherever there is an opening for a gush-
ing widow with a frock cut low at the front, The
Bulletin cordially recommends this lady for the
billet.
** * *
A company, actuated doubtless by urgent
private reasons, broke out on Saturday at the
Sydney Opera House with “ A Foundered For-
tune.’’ There is an element of absolute originality
about this drama, a wild unexpectedness, a re-
gardlessness of time and space, and a tendency to
be reckless about chronology, which are very re-
markable. It begins with the return of an old
gentleman suspected of murder, who has been
hiding for 15 years, and his nephew, who is
alleged to be a “pirate,” lays poison for him,
only his principal enemy drinks it instead, and
when he dies a strange woman rushes in, and for
no visible reason denounces the old man’s son-in-
law. The son-in-law is sentenced to death, and
the nephew proves to be someone else, and
the proofs of all this are understood to be
hidden in an iron safe which can’t be opened
because a boy has vainly offered the keys to
everybody and no one will take them. Then the
escaped son-in-law and the safe and the whole
company go to sea in a coffin-ship, and the son is
stunned and put in the safe, and it sinks to the
bottom of the deep. The interest centres princi-
pally in that safe. Wlien it is recovered by divers
weeks later, and brought back to the office, Frank
Cates hangs about it with a pickaxe but fears to
open it because his squashed and putrefied victim
will roll out as an accusing mass of old green
decay. But when he does open it the old gentle-
man trots out instead, and the drowned victim
steps in through the doorway and accuses Cates, in
voice, of many crimes. Whereupon
the latter gentleman blows himself up by firing
the gas-meter. _ In a previous conflict Cates is
shot five times in the feet but escapes uninjured
Also an ardent lover allows his girl to be
locked up in the cellar with Cates, and hears that
gentleman attacking her with the pickaxe without
remonstrance, A small newsboy proves to be a
baronet because he is the son of a lord, which is
about as clear as if his uncle had fallen overboard
and consequently his grandfather was a sledge-
hammer. I owards the end a horrible suspicion
arises that Mr. Cates is the safe, but is trving to
disguise himself as the pickaxe, and that'the gas
explosion is the Dora Dora blacks. A certain
amount of decent acting is wasted on this per-
formance. Misses Fannie Thynne and Ada
.Lawrence are two attractive ladies. Mr. J. P.
West is a pleasant Hebrew with a rich oid nose
and a voice which is awash with unctuousness and
port wine; and Mr. Cates presents certain good
points. 8

y ears of thereabouts since
Human Nature ’ was last seen in Sydney, and
it is still the same big, brassy drama as of old.
1 hemostconspicuous idea of the authors, evidently
is that, when a man finds his wife struggling to
escape from a person who holds her by the curls
or otherwise, hxs natural and reasonable course,
instead of jumping on the assailant, is to cast the
lady orr and go away to hide his misery in the
ooudan. And his next fixed conviction seems to
be that when a lawyer wants to track a brutally
ill-used victim, he quite naturally runs her down
with dogs, or any other means, in open daylight, or
at any other time, and that he has full power to
arrest her by the back button of tier frock wherever
he finds her. Also, there is a general impression
that when anybody dies the money naturally goes
to the nearest scoundrel, and that when a family
solicitor is authorised to take divorce proceedings
against a lady, his first act, as a matter i
course, is to tear her infant from her by ha a
and carry it away. In fact, “Human Nature'l
is built on two principal assumptions; first th f
a lawyer is practically omnipotent; and seeonH
that, in free and happy England, ladies of hilt
descent are habitually dragged around by th
scalp, and chased through bogs, briars, and dart
forests, and chivvied at night over railway areh '
by their legal representatives. It is an 'awfll
commentary on British institutions— the
of that pale, bedraggled lady, with her stocking
torn and muddy, and her dress rent asunder hat
less, hungry, and seared, racing till she drum
through swamps and hedges with her child under
her arm, and the solicitor in full cry behind her
This discrepancy about the solicitor is the chief
objection to “Human Nature,” which, apart from
its legal aberrations, is an interesting composition
Bland Holt makes a comic law clerk such as m
attorney every had in his office before or since and
W, E. Baker, Sass, Corlesse, and one or two more
are not so overwhelmed by the scenery as to alto
gether lose their individuality. Also, Mrs. Bland
Holt impersonates a particularly loveable little
nurse, and the rest of the co. maintains a reason-
able level of respectability.

The Tivoli, under Harry Rickards’ manage-
ment, remains superior to the general depression
of Sydney. A new London craze, “Daddy
wouldn’t buy me a Bow-wow,” is promised for
Saturday.
** * *
“It’s Never Too Late to Mend" con-
tinues a profitable career at Sydney Majesty’s
and George Rignold promises to inaugurate a
series of “ souvenir nights,” which, he says will
come off “ without loss of dignity or prestige to
this popular theatre.” It all depends, in The
Bulletin’s opinion, on what the souvenirs coni
sist of. The present writer distinctly remembers
seeing an aggravated bushman kicking Ms
souvenir to flinders (it was a double perambulator)
in the stalls of a Brisbane theatre, and when ho
had dashed the ruins of it into the orchestra he
offered to fight the whole company, and then
stalked out leaving a trail of objurgations behind
him. That sort of thing will have to he guarded
against, and disappointed souvenirists removed
promptly by the police. “ Shamus O’Brien,” at
the Royal, will be succeeded on Saturday by
“ The Black Flag.” Shamus will die sincerely
regretted.
** $ «
Fitzgeralds’ Circus was blessed on Wed-
nesday, last week, with the special patronage o*
Lady Duff and the viceregal family, in conse-
quence of which the hippodrome may now be
regarded as an entertainment which no genuine
society person can stay away from without loss of
caste. The immediate result of the gubernatorial
patronage was that more people couldn’t get in
than usual, and that the disappointed hundreds
were more vociferous outside the tent than pre-
viously. Meanwhile, it is gratifying to know that
Commodore distinctly approves of Lady Duff, and
that the biggest lion seems of opinion that she will
prove a social success. Also, the rest of the
animals regarded her and the family with a
friendly eye.
*% * *
George Dean’s youthful opera company is
doing good business with the “ Black Cloaks” at
Sydney Gaiety. It is becoming more and more
evident that this enterprise intends to be a suc-
cess. One at least of the characters whom The
Bulletin dropped upon with fatherly severity in
“ Rip Van Winkle ” has evidently profited by the
admonition, and those who were commended by
this paper still deserve commendation. Miss
Cynthia Carey and Miss Marie Dunoon in parti-
cular are two operatic planets that are becoming
increasingly visible above the horizon, and the
notes of the last-named lady’s voice should be
declared legal tender throughout New South
Wales if the State has any semblance of music in
its soul.
** * *
Sundry misguided characters arose at
Sydney Royal Standard on Saturday and pro-
ceeded to play “ Neck for Neck,” a sky-rending
shriek in which one member of the company is
deservedly hanged in full view of the audience,
and afterwards goes mad with a red gash on his
neck where the rope used to be. Apart from the
hanging, it is a miserable drama, more incoherent
even than Isaiah Cohen’s novel; and the ignorance
of the writer is like unto a high shot-tower keeping
watch over the desert. It might, perhaps, take a
little better if the hangee were more efficiently
suspended. The Bulletin hereby suggests that
the management should borrow Smedley, the
murderer —who is to be hanged anyhow, ana
might be hanged as comfortably at the Standard
as anywhere else—and suspend him in solemn
reality. Half the profits of the first night mign
go to the wife of the deceased as some small com-
fort in her bereavement and an alleviation ot he
sorrow. Then the diseased and wretched dp®
would at least have done some little good m i
time. N.8.-—We just remember that Smed y
hasn’t any wife ; he had one but he killed her.

Fifteen years back she was famous in her parti-
cular line of art, and her present interpretations
of the old songs are fifteen years better. She
will come as a great surprise to “colonials”
who may have heard her often in England
long ago. Her fine, remarkable contralto is rounder
n | fuller, in keeping with Madame’s more
tragedy-queenly appearance; and the effects she
nmduoes with the same words are rounder and
fuller likewise. “The Genevieve Ward of the
concert stage” would be a suitable title for
Antoinette Sterling to-day.

At the Princess’s they are still prospering
with the double bill of “Trial by Jury” an‘*
“Vicar of Bray.” The firstnamed, although stal e
to the frequenters of benefit matinees, is quite a
novelty to the general evening public, and the
court’s wild method of settling a breach of promise
case seems to fill a long-felt want. The comic
opera season has but another week or two to run.
Marching orders have been issued, and the immor-
tal ballet is drawing to a close.
#* * *
“White Slaves” was produced at Mel’
bourne Royal on Saturday, “for one night only.”
After the third act, when three lots of policemen
had effected three distinct arrests at the same
aivful moment, and the whole visible drama was
writhing in the iron grip of the Law, there was a
sustained shriek for the author, J. J. Utting. As
a natural result of this demonstration the author
had, at time of writing, decided to carry “ White
Slaves” on for an indefinite period, and possibly
it is still on view. A villain of the basest descrip-
tion occupies the centre of this great literary pro-
duction. He keeps 19 inferior characters at bay for
about three hours. At the finish a dying boy
pulls himself together with his parting breath and
shouts the villain, by which time the comic publi-
can has also expired, and the production of certain
papers by a previous corpse enables the innocent
girl to marry the convict. The other girl
has been betrayed in the meantime, and is
doing well, so everything ends happily, although
the fate of a certain adventuress remains in doubt.
Possibly this lady joins herself in wedlock to the
Jew “sweater,” and ultimately chokes him with
a slop-vest at ninepence the dozen for the making
up. The Hebrew in question is undeserving of a
better fate. He keeps a “den” of nice, clean,
sowing-girls, who claim that they are “above
being tempted.” These are White Slaves, but
fortunately their spirit is not quite crushed for
want of vittles. In an unguarded moment the
greasy son of Israel proposes to violate the sanctity
of his den by kissing one of the fair Circassians,
whereupon they all rise as one slave and smite
him with bundles of “washing.” He is next
thrown out of a window, and the poor, toiling
virgins mostly take to the stage as a last resource.
The den is only one of many exciting incidents in
the course of a remarkable play, but it divides
chief honours with a certain “red room,” where-
unto the villain, during a long dishonourable life-
time, orders his principal accusers to be conveyed.
* &
Amongst other Melbourne shows (mostly
in a despondent state), the newest is a Garden of
Eden, at the Rotunda Hall. The home of our
First Parents is composed of three large mirrors
and some rural decorations. A stranger entering
the garden sees himself repeated in an endless
number of similar festive spots, therefore the
beauty of the scene depends a good deal upon the
appearance of the parties who have paid to go in.
It is a clever idea, carried out on a not very
expensive scale. If the promoter is an agent for
Gardens of Eden, and is showing this as a sample,
he ought to take a few orders notwithstanding the
depression. To take one’s best girl into this sim-
ple illusion is bliss indeed, for her engaging smile
fascinates right on through the groves of Time,
and you are with her evermore, so to speak.

Various rumours having been circulated
as to the coming production of “ The Black
Crook,” it may be as well to say that Messrs.
Williamson and Musgrove have, for three months
past, had this piece in preparation. Their artists
are now at work on it, and a very large sum has
been spent. Mr. Musgrove has engaged some
important novelties, including several splen-
did dancers from the Continent, and has recently
returned to London from New York whither he
went specially to see the American production.
Ihe Black Crook ” will be the biggest and most
costly thing of the kind yet attempted in Aus-
tralia.

And then La Perseveranza, March 18: —More imposing
demonstrations of admiration, and greater manifestations
of enthusiasm than those which Madame Nellie Melba
obtained last evening at the Scala, singing before an
extraordinary select and crowded audience, I do not think
any artist could aspire to in those rosy dreams of glory
which are so often transformed into bitter delusions. . .
The famous artist appeared visibly moved by this pro-
longed applause as well as by the numerous offers of
splendid flowers in bouquets and baskets which were
handed to her on the stage at this moment. Few artists
can lay claim to having obtained such an uncontested and
triumphal success on the stage of La Scala. She also
conquered the public yesterday evening with the beauty
of her figure, to which are added the attractions of a
graceful and lofty carriage, and the noble expression of
her face, framed with an abundant wealth of hair with
strange changing shades of burnished gold, tints from the
Titianesque pallet, assisting greatly the other charms of
the artist. _ For ease of production, bright as precious
stones flashing in the sun, firmness of utterance, ductility
of timbre, sureness and audacious confidence in her vocal
means, for finish in modulation and coloratura, for true
artistic insight which—notwithstanding shakes, excep-
tional trills and acrobatic flights—does not deface or spoil
the purest manifestations of Donizetti’s genius, the cele-
brated artist made one perceive that she had reached the
highest pitch of perfection.

Dear Bulletin,—You suggest that the Crown
(in Victoria) is making a display of severity by “ jumping
on Matt. Larkin ” once more. The liquidators in Larkin’s
building society are the real movers in this case, and the
Crown, being obviously unable to assume the convict s
innocence, could not avoid taking further proceedings
when requested. It is a charge of forgery that they are
now bringing against Larkin. South Melbourne. [The
“ Crown ” could “ obviously enter a nolle prosequi for
Larkin as easily as for Davies. The “ Crown " can assume
anybody’s innocence in Victoria—if it wants to.—Ed. B.J

B. J. Fink’s halfpenny in the <£ composition
was staled for just what it was worth, without reserve—
as far as The Bulletin knows. There were no false pre-
tences about the distribution of that small, but interest-
ing, coin of British extraction. Since B.J.’s time
numerous Melbourne men have “got through’ with a
penny—less or more—on the strength of private pro-
mises that the smaller, or non-land-boom, creditors would
presently be paid in full. And now the parties who
accepted a penny and a promise are, in many cases,
whistling in vain for the promise to be fulfilled. Judge
Molesworth had been notified of this confidence-trick
when he suddenly refused to pass any more copper com-
positions. Asthe old song doesn’t say: For ’tis easy to
give the penny where the 19s. lid. will never be.
• * »
The relations of the Australia Hotel Co. and
the Anglo-Australian Land and Building Co. are a be-
wildering complication. The Anglo-Australian spent
most of its capital and reserves in building the really
palatial hotel, which proved more than it could swallow.
In fact, the caravanserai was almost the bigger enterprise
of the two, and when it was finished the situation was
very like that of the snake which has swallowed a bullock ;
there wasn’t enough snake to cover the bullock, and it
persisted in sticking out at the end. Then, owing to a
variety of tilings, it was resolved to float the hotel into
a nominally separate company, but, as the Anglo-Austra-
lian held 290,000 out of 300,000 shares it was practically
the same company over again. Of these shares 150,000
were fully paid up, and 140,000 were contributing. The
Anglo-Australian also held a £lOO,OOO second mortgage
over the hotel, so that things were tolerably mixed. Then
the financial institution burst, and, the mortgage falling
due about the same time, the liquidator demanded pay-
ment. This was where the fiendish vengeance of the
hotel co. came in. It acknowledged the £lOO,OOO liability
and made a call of £1 per share to meet it, consequently
the Anglo-Australian’s share of the call was just £140,000.
Not being able to meet this little demand the hotel co.
confiscated the mortgage in part payment, and then pro-
ceeded to dun the miserable bankrupt Anglo-Australian
for the £40,000 balance. This also being unobtainable,
the next step is naturally to sell the Anglo-Australian’s
140,000 shares for unpaid calls, and, as the market for this
stock is already depressed, and as a big forced sale will
inevitably make the bottom fall out of it entirely, the
chances are that the whole lot won’t realise £40,000, or
anything like it. Therefore, a perfectly possible result,
so far as we can make out, is that the Anglo-Australian,
through trying to realise its £lOO,OOO mortgage will lose
the mortgage, will lose its 140,000 shares, and will still
owe a balance which may yet absorb the 150,000 paid-up
shares as well.

Matthew Davies was chief friend and patron of
James Mi rams when that enterprising little financier
financed himself into a hopeless mess. But for unseen
circumstances, Davies would now, one presumes, be lead-

to him. James Service has undertaken to look after this
matter, however. Mirams is a lucky man in his cham-
pions. But he was unlucky to get into prison. His
building society went bung a couple of years too soon.
That’s all.
