« # *
The assiduously-worked job conferring upon a
far-reaching syndicate the use of the rapidly flowing
waters of the Colo and Grose Valley for electric-power-
generating purposes has, as we write, almost passed the
N.S.W. Legislature. The promoters will be able to float
this right in London for an enormous sum. It could just
as well have been floated by the State, which then could
have put into the Treasury the big profits that must
now go into the pockets of the Hall crowd—you can
also spell it Haul. This, with the passing of the Simpson’s
railway job by the same Legislature, is another feature of
the fast-dying session. The Bulletin, marking by how
many soi-disant democrats in Parliament and journalism
these nefarious schemes have been openly and secretly
supported, and knowing the arguments by which their
support has been secured, almost despairs of the future
of honest government.
m m m
Seemingly Matthew Davies is not to be arrested
by cablegram at Colombo. A detective is going after him,
however, with the sure and certain hope of getting there
a week after Davies is gone. Meanwhile, it begins to
appear as if the Victorian authorities very nearly failed
to be too late to arrest Matthew' at Adelaide ; they sent
their telegram when it seemed almost certain the Salier
was gone, but the vessel was behind time, and thus a
horrid accident nearly happened. Also, there is some
peculiar hitch about the Grand Jury which will probably
end by getting Matthew off on a technical point if every-
thing else fails.
* # #
In the boom, many Melbourne and Sydney
brokers, probably with a view of encouraging business,
published diagrams, indicating the rise of shares. For
instance, the rise in Melbourne trams was shown by a red
line carried up to the top of a page ; so with Broken Hill
and other well-known stocks. The writer has waited to
find a broker with pluck enough to present the other side
of the picture. It might be called the downfall diagram.

The B.IST.S.W. notes have now their respective
amounts marked in blue pencil because of the indistinct-
ness of the printed figures. Notes printed in haste may
be forged at leisure. The N.S.W. Treasury-notes are also
easily forgeable.

How fishy are the surface revelations made in the
estate of the successful Sydney absconder, John Mont-
gomery Fogarty ! And what a flutter there will be in a
certain dovecote should the whole matter be probed !
• * *
The acquisition of a fortune is not quite every-
thing in this world. For instance, there is in Australia a man
who undoubtedly promoted one of the biggest and most
successful mining swindles in history. Well, Nature has
taken her revenge, and even his bitterest enemy or most
wretched victim now has pity on him.
* * *
A Sydney draper who lately reduced wages has
drawn up a statement of his affairs for the last three
years, which, having been compiled only for his own
information and that of his friends, may.be taken as
bona-fide. It shows that the ground landlord has eaten
up all the profits throughout that term. The ground-
rent is £4OOO a year. The original landlord bought the
block for £650, but the tenant is threatened with a 60 per
cent, increase as soon as the lease falls in.
• * «
Kelly’s Block promises to be a live mine at last.
Just now it is almost the only moving thing on the
Towers Exchange.
* * *
Market mining is dead, and there is reason to
hope that it will never be revived on the Australian boom-
ing basis. Necessity will make men who have been living
on bluff or confidence work for a living, and as hitherto
laziness in Si dney begat laziness' in the mine, so now hard
work will beget hard work. Already the mineral fields of
N.S.W. are being raked over, and discarded lOdwt. reefs
are yielding more than tucker to tributors and others.
Poor as are the great bulk of the N.S.W. reefs, there are
many which -will pay when well and systematically
worked.
# * *
Judge Harding (Q.) rules that a mining co. may
legally mine under freehold property, but that the Crown
may demand its royalty. A freeholder owns his freehold
down to thecentre of the earth, with everything contained
therein, except the royal metals, which Include gold
bearing quartz. The decision is momentous, but need;
a Privy Council approval.

Mills’ Day Dawn United, one of the big mines
of the Towers, has in three years crushed 63,748 tons, for
61,5640 z. gold, and declared £75,000 in dividends. Capital
£300,000 ; its present dividend representing 32 per cent,
on capital.
# # •
The word “lode” is now introduced into the
Mills United report. The term, deceptive in this connec-
tion, we think, has not before been used on the Towers.
The United is eternally advertising ns a fact that the
supply of stone is illimitable, whereas the fortnightly
reports show nothing of consequence below the No. 6
level. Twenty-four and sixpence is, contends a corres-
pondent, an extravagant price for the shares in a co. of
300,000, and at the rate stone is.being raised, the present
supply can’t keep up for ever. The mine is certainly a
big producer and a really good property, but a false
impression has gone abroad respecting the quantity of
stone in sight.

The Brilliant (employing over 200 men) is
largely adopting the contract system. The gradual
decline in the yield per ton is making dividends small, and,
as at Broken Hill, the men must suffer.

The Victory moved its account (about ,£25,000)
from the A.J.S. to the B. of N.S.W. only a few days
before the smash. Managing-director Buckland was then
in Sydney. He is a nephew of the B.NJ3.W. chairman
and brother of the successful C. J. Buckland—who, by
the way, managed the B.N.S.W. branch at Gympie in the
early days.
« * *
The splendid Day Dawn reef, so far as the P.C.
and the Block are concerned, is now almost a memory.
These two mines must have paid something near to a
million in dividends. Manager Buckland, of the P.C.,
with his usual luck, struck, some two or three months ago,
a big reef underlying the old Day Dawn, and in the same
deep straight shaft went through several smaller reefs
very rich in gold, so this claim promises to have a re-
newed life.
# * *
Great was the fall of the syndicate at the late
Towers election. They took their thrashing badly, and
many of those active on the Labour side have since re-
ceived short notice. The attitude of the miners utterly
deceived the clique. Silence was read as consent and not
und°rstond as fear of being marked men ; consequently,
the syndicate agents went down terribly in wagers.
* * *
It is reasonably safe to infer now when an im-
portant development of carbonate ore, in a Broken Hill
mine, is announced, that some wretched little pocket has
been struck which would not have excised more than pass-
ing notice in the days of the boom. The carbonate era,
as far as the Broken Hill line of lode is concerned, is a
thing of the past.

The concentrating plant lately supplied by the
Parke and Lacy Co., Limited, Sydney, for the Silver King
Silver Mining Co., Zeehan (Tas.), is giving the greatest
satisfaction. The plant is putting through 200 tons
weekly, working but 16 hours per day, and the Frue
vanners are demonstrating their value there as they have
done in so many other places.

Eight months ago a number of Sydney account
ants formed themselves into a sort of Trades Protection
Society, and a considerable amount was subscribed in
ten-guinea entrance-fees. Earnest enquiries are being
made by some of the subscribers as to the fate of the
undertaking. Perhaps the society is in course of recon-
struction. _

The Kentish mine, Bendigo, out of which Paddy
Watson took several tons of gold, was lately sold to the
Carlisle Co., whose mine adjoins, for £3OOO. A cake of
2600 z., got last month, is proof that Paddy did not take
it all out.
As exciting as a bank rush, or a fire panic,
is the headlong struggle of competitive customers
daily witnessed at the well-stocked emporium of
David Jones and Co., opposite the General Post
Office, George-street, Sydney, where snug winter
apparel, that has come into popular approval
under the unassuming title of “Orient Clothing,”
is being sold at rates that represent only the
genuine cost price. The name of David Jones
and Co. gives the hall-mark of superior utility and
choice elegance to any of its merchandise, and the
prices are now well within reach of ordinary
purses.

and-butter is cut for life —as the old lawyer said

Parkes is not dead yet as was reported.
The venerable politician has again had to sleep on
the door-mat lately.
A West Coast (M.L.) editor describes his
contemporary as a person who “ masquerades in
the trousers of a journalist.”
In the Speight-AGE libel-action Purves
and Deakin, defendant’s leading counsel, each
receives 750 guineas on his brief.
Gov. Hopetoun, with his rather roseate
moustache, looks more than ever like a counter-
hand at Robertson and Moffat’s.

N.S.W. Land Court, and now again at the Bar,
was the first LL.B. of Sydney University.

in S.A., says he has lost £BO,OOO in squatting
during the last 30 years, but has still a little left.

On Parkes’ 78th birthday (27th May) John
Davies assembled to congratulate him. The agony
of it ! Almost 80 and congratulated by John
Davies !
At a Caledonian gathering in Melb., a few
years ago, the boiler of whisky-punch was stirred
with a rafter from the cabin where Burns was
born.
Gov. Duff’ smiles at Mint-master Cameron’s
kiltie enthusiasm. The latter suggested that the
Governor and he should both honour Scotland on
occasions of social display.
Janies Service, M.L.O. (Melb.), says he is
now 70 years of age, and has never had any-
thing to do with a company unless he had a voice
in its management.
The Age leader-man is very familiar with
Soott. He compares the Argus to “Roderick
Dhu challenging an army —‘ Come one, come all.’ ”
Very nice, but Fitz James was the party.
The two “ Bills,” Maloney and levers,
Victorian M.’sP., are much addicted to ringing
in the Deity in their oratory. “ God only knows,”
etc., is nearly first cousin to “ Be me sowl!”
Byron Moore, of the V.R.C., says that
when he was Black Wednesday’d by the Berry
Government he could have earned his living as
an engineer or a compositor. His father made
him learn both arts.
Gov. Duff lingered longer over his first
introduction to Edward Bulwer Lytton Dickens
than over any two of his other visitors at Govern-
ment House, last week. The Governor and
Dickens pere were intimates years ago, and they
continued good friends even after the caricaturist’s
pen had dealt broadly with the Scotchman’s
mannerisms.
Proverbial as is tha wealth of
the Rothschilds, says an Australa-
sian correspondent, no member of
that family is anything like so rich
as Lord Iveagh. His fortune, by
far the largest in Great Britain,
amounts to £14,000,000; of tins
£8,000,000 represents his share in
Guinness’s Brewery. He has besides
£2,000,000 in London and North-
western railway stock. He never
touches speculative ventures of any
kind. When he and his brother
determined to float Guinness’s as a
company, they first took the pro-
posal to'the Rothschilds, suggesting
£8,000,000 as the amount of capital
to be offered to the public. Roths-
childs said that anything above
£3,000,000 would be exorbitant,
and would not be subscribed.
The Guinnesses then took the
proposal to Baring Brothers,
who floated thebrewery
at once as a limited co.
with immense success.
The Guinness concern
is worth £24,000,000 at
present quotations. But
in the end this wonder-
ful coup was the ruin
of the Baring firm. It
completely turned Lord
Revelstoke’s head. The
partners thenceforward
imagined that every-
thing they touched
must turn to gold, and
they straightway
plunged head over heels
into the Argentine
quicksand. -All these
Guinness millions, of
course, are derived
from the inimitable merits of Dublin stout, the
whole thing depending on a slight creamy flavour
which remains after each “gulp” of this classic
beverage.
The special jury in the Speight v. the
Age case comprises one tentmaker, builder, agent,
estate agent, blacksmith, jeweller, ironmonger,
importer, butcher, publican, and two printers. A
very careful assortment.
Last week, through a bank-smash, mil-
lionaire Tyson had a cheque returned him. Later
on, Jimmy had to borrow a pound from an
acquaintance in Pittsworth, a township close to
Felton, his Darling Downs property.

Chancellor Brownless, of Melb. University,
and victim of a recent accident, writes his own
bulletins to the papers. Hence the exaggerated
statements as to bis condition, and the consequent
anger of his medical attendants.
Anyone who cares to advertise in Mel-
bourne now that he has for sale a complete file of
a certain Castlemaine paper of about 40 years ago,
would probably receive £lOO or so from a pro-
minent politician to say no more about it.
N.S.W. Attorney-General Barton favours
the passing of an Act rendering it illegal for the
relative, whether by consanguinity or affinity, of
the presiding Judge or magistrate, to appear as
solicitor or barrister in prosecution or defence.
“If you say ‘Commonwealth’ in Latin
you say ‘Republic,’” said Patrick Jennings, in
N.S.W. Upper House, last week. Which reminds
one that the ex-Premier is a moderately-proficient
Latin scholar and an exceptionally good Italian
one.
Millidge was scrupulously careful to inform
the Vic. Crown officials of his intention to leave
Melbourne for Adelaide, “on a visit.” What he
apparently forgot to inform them of was his inten-
tion to leave Adelaide for England, on another
visit.
Only a few years ago Matthias Larkin, the
South Melbourne Building Society secretary, now
“doing” six years for a lot of frauds, and
committed for some more, recovered £5O damages
from a newspaper for venturing to doubt his
word.
Parson Buchanan, of Brisbane (Q.), has
just got back from a missionary begging expedi-
tion to Britain, with £9OO. It cost £450 to hire
a parson to take bis place in bis absence, and his
expenses were £449 ; total profit for the “ pore
heathen,” 20s.
Robert Adams, who died of small-pox the
other day at Perth (W.A.), was three times sub-
jected to vaccination, but the vaccine lymph
wouldn’t take. Some doctors thereupon told him
that he was evidently smallpox-proof. He doesn’t
believe in doctors now.
Speaker Abbott is sometimes something
more than ornamental. He erased a question,
the other night, which would have shown that a
member of the House confounded Greville, of
“ Creville’s Memoirs ” with the democratically-
inclined legislator of N.S.W. Upper House.
The new Edinburgh Review touches on
Astronomer Proctor’s wretched death. The New
York landlord packed him off to the hospital for
yellow fever, whereas it was only malarial fever.
He died in 24 hours, and would have been spared
with proper treatment. It recalls Edgar Poe.
The day following Cambooya (Q.) election,
when “Jonah” Perkins was ousted by Labour-
man Daniels, a wag called at Perkins’ hotel and
sent up a message: “Mr. Daniels’ compliments,
and will yer let him have his railway-pass?”
What Perkins said was not reported—on earth.
Madame Antoinette Sterling has a voice
like an angel and a face like a putty mask. She
is a cold-water advocate, and was feted by the
Women’s Christian Temperance Union when in
Adelaide. She has never worn stays or used a
hair-pin—except, perhaps, to button her gloves.

born in a stable.” Had Crick not been a modest
man he would have gone farther back than Keats.

Government Astronomer Ellery (Vic.) once
got a letter during the regime of Governor Hotham
dispensing with his services. In the same envelope
was another letter raising his salary. That is
nearly 40 years ago, and, up to the present, the
star-gazer has never discovered an explanation of
the curious incident.
Lieutenanfc-Qpvernor Way (S.A.) oilers to
take Lord Kintore’s place for a year for <£4ooo.
Thus will be saved the Chief Justice’s salary of
£2OOO, and £lOOO of the vice - regal income.
Further, the little man undertakes to spend all
the money in sociality, and he is famed for his
skill as an entertainer.
William Shea, of Pambula (N.S.W.),
who has been resident there for quite half a
century, is the grandson of that Capt. Shea who
commanded the transport Scarborough, which
arrived in Botany Bay with “the First Fleet,” in
1788. Shea is a N.S.W. native, having been born
in Clarence-street, Sydney, in ’24.
Marcus Clarke’s newly-issued novel, “ Chi-
diock Tichbourne,” is by no means rubbish. A
blend of Scott, Dumas, and James, it only lacks
the actuality of “ His Natural Life,” and is
bookish, with all the staccato of Clarke’s news-
paper style. His friends are pleased with the
book, the fault being that it is too short.
Advice to Young Men Dr. Richards’ ad., p. 1.
Queen Victoria sympathised with the Aus-
tralian warriors—penniless, owing to their bank
having smashed—to such an extent that she —sent
each of them a portrait of herself to console him: —
The warrior bowed his crested head,
And tamed his awful spleen,
And gazed upon the gift, and said :
“ That’s very like the Queen ! ”
A German medico of parts, at present
‘ dead-beat,” was summoned in Melbourne last
week for stealing a skull, the property of the
Publio Library. He borrowed the skull, saying
lie was going to lecture on phrenology, but the
desire for a meal induced him to pawn the relic.
The poor doctor, however, is not much worse off
than the journalist reduced to the extremity of
pawning his own brains.
Parkes last week stated in the N.S.W. As-
sembly that in 1874 he was “a mere boy.” Parkes
was a guileless child of CO in those days, and used to
stand off his creditors on the plea of infancy. The
present writer distinctly remembers finding him
crying in the Domain about that time, and taking
him home. He had a short frock on, and he was
boo-hooing because he had dropped his toffee in
the mud.

Ben. Douglass,
ex-president of Mel-
bourne Trades Hall
Council, is accus-
tomed to declare
that not one single
leader of the big
strike ever did a
day’s hard work.
But he is wrong.
When the boot-
trade went to smash
some years ago,
Trenwith turned up
on the wharf one
day, and sought a
job stevedoring.
The boss of the
gang didn’t think
him worth much,
and told him so,
but took him on.
The wharf-labourers
had seen greenhorns
tackle their work
before, and waited
to see this one cave
in. But Trenwith
didn’t — he went
right on, and when,
at the close of the
day, he held out his
torn and bleeding
hand for his pay,
the boss roughly
complimented him
on his pluck. “ I
aske'd for a day’s
work,” said Tren-
with, “ and I think
I’ve done it.” He
had ; but he hasn’t
tried stevedoring
since.
A man wearing a soft felt hat, a dingy
coat all unbuttoned, no waistcoat, a blue shirt on
full parade duty, and slop trousers, waited at the
Q. railway commissioners’ office the other day.
The, messenger took him up abruptly with a
“What is it?” “Is the secretary in?” “Yes;
what d’you want with him?” “I want to see
him.” “What’s your business?” “Tell him,
please, I have come about my pass.” “Oh, no,
you don’t ; we don’t have anything to do with
passes here—you must go to the Labour Bureau.
“Well, you’d better tell the secretary that ”
but the finish is better told by explaining that the
waistcoatless man was wharf-lumper Turley, M.P.
for South Brisbane, on the warpath for his rail-
way-pass, clad in full working uniform.
A correspondent: — “I only saw Matt.
Davies’ mouth and chin once. Until May, 1891,
he wore a long, yellow beard, and was reputed
handsome. The society papers coupled him and
Jim Shaokell, M.L.A., of Rodney, as the two
handsomest men in the Vie. Assembly. Well,
Shaokell never shaved, but Matthew Henry did
and the operation disclosed the weak, flabby,
sensual mouth familiar to people who have studied
the composite photos, of which Havelock Ellis is
so fond. Davies was too pachydermatous ever to
leave Victoria for Colombo because surgeon Fitz.
feared acute inflammation of the brain might set
in. His stupid mouth and prognathous jaw gave
him away exactly three months before the City of
Melbourne Building Society went bung in August,
1891, being the first of his packet of squibs.”
Old Col. Smith, of Ballarat, never did
much work with his muscles, but he mostly got
the Labour-vote. C. E. Jones was once opposing
him and running him close in the Sebastopol dis-
trict, where the Taffies were strongly inclined to
go solid for the man with the Welsh name. But
at a big meeting, the night before the poll, the
Colonel turned up in an aggressively new suit of
very shaggy tweed. That night, he said, he had
the honour to stand before the Welshmen of Sebas-
topol in a suit of tweed made in Sebastopol by a
Welsh weaver. The day was not far distant when
the whole of Victoria would be wearing Sebas-
topol tweed, and when the fame of its Welsh
weavers would resound through the land. Next
day the Taffy vote went solid for Smith —but they
never again saw him in that shaggy tweed suit.
The late G. D. Langridge, M.L.A. (Vic.,)
sometime Commissioner of Public Works, had a
pretty wit. One day a deputation from Burra-
budgery waited upon him concerning the necessity
for the erection of a gaol in the town. The chief
of the legation was particularly effusive in the
matter, so much so that Langridge, turning to
another ambassador close by, earnestly asked:
“ Why is he so anxious? Does he expect to be
run in soon himself?” After listening to all the.
oratory, Langridge, without a smile, said : “Gen-
tlemen, I knew very little about your town before,
but lam now convinced that it has a future. 1
have now made the acquaintance of a number ol
the town’s leading citizens (the deputation bowed),
and, after meeting them, though unable to state
exactly what the Government will do, I may say
that I am now fully convinced that in your town a
new gaol is urgently needed.” The deputation
left, wondering why the reporters giggled.
King O’Malley, a well-known Adelaide
(S. A.) insurance-drummer, broke out into the fol-
lowing oratorical measles in connection with a
recent aiding-distress movement: —
Are there no ladies in this garden city where every day
the maiden’s morning blush is being kissed by the per-
fumed breezes fresh from the classic heights of Lofty,
who have sufficient independence of character and
stability of mind to emulate their American sisters and
save the many unfortunate people that are now suffering
from the degradation of the possibility of beggary? Are
the beautiful rivers of South Australia to be permitted lo
flow to the broad bosom of the ocean swollen by the tears
of gentle woman’s wretchedness, weeping over hungry
infants on the withered breast of want ?
In Sydney, it sounds like a sensuous ecstaoy
of Danyelloconnorian yum-yum, and not an all-
round cadge.
A VERBATIM REPORT.
CABBY (pulling up at corner and addressing 13-year-old youngster): “Say,
sonny, what would your father say if he saw you smoking that
burnin’ mountain, hey?”
THE YOUNG AUSTRALIA : “I don’t care a blank blank what the old
blank says! Not me! I earn more blanky wages than the
blanky old man does, and he blanky well knows it. He ain’t
blanky likely to say anything while I supports the family.
Drive on, Cabby, and make yer bloomin’ fortune.”
Chief Justice Madden, who originally drew
up the Age’s defence to Speight’s libel-action, was
spoken of as likely to hear the case in Melb.
Supreme Court. The Law never makes allowances
for human nature. If a murderer confessed his
crime confidentially for the better “ instruction ”
of counsel, and if said counsel were suddenly con-
verted into a judge, his Honor, doubtless, would
be a fit and proper person to sit in judgment on
his former candid client, and direct the jury with
an unbiassed mind.
“ Lord Derby was never drunk, but drank
too much every day for 50 years.” Happy man !
The Prince of Wales and the Baked
'Tater Man.
The following story is told by the Prince of
Wales and the baked “ tater ” man: One night
lately the Prince of Wales and the Duke of
Edinburgh were returning home rather late.
When they passed Joe, the well-known “ tater ”
man - in the Pall Mall, they took a fancy to taste
his wares, and were accommodated by Joe with a
red-hot baked potato from the can, for which
they paid a penny each, and proceeded on their
way. Joe had recognised his customers, and on
the next day his “ tater ” can was decorated with
a glaring gilt sign showing the three feathers and
the legend “Purveyor to H.M.S. the Prince of
Wales.” He stationed his apparatus opposite
Marlborough House, to the Prince’s mingled
amusement and dismay. The Prince sent an
equerry to order him away, but Joe refused to
budge, and kept on doing a rushing business. At
last the Prince ordered Sir Francis Knollys to buy
him off, and the feathers were removed. This
shows the Prince to be a patron of “baked taters.”
We wonder if he suffers from liver complaint—
most stout people do; they feel a tired, languid
feeling in the morning, the urine is high-coloured
and scalds. They are constipated, suffer with
sick headaches, and feel generally miserable, but
there is no excuse, for a remedy—we refer to Clem-
ents Tonic —is within reach of all, and should at
once be resorted to or serious results must even-
tuate, and perhaps life be sacrificed, at the least
the disease will increase till a dangerous state is
reached. Take a lesson from the case of Mr.
Charles E. Howard, Royal Hotel, Morpeth. N.S.
Wales, who writes on February 3rd, 1893:—I
have since early school-d ays been more or less a
victim to the most excruciating pains in the chest,
attributed to chronic indigestion ; they caused me
no end of trouble and pains and even anxiety;
restlessness in my sleep, accompanied by the most
horrid dreams; each morning brought forth a
more languid feeling than the last. Having tried
all kinds of remedies without good effect, I was at
last induced to try Clements Tonic. After taking
three bottles of this wonderful health-giving
remedy a complete change came over my system,
for I feel I am now perfectly cured. I am only
too happy to add my testimony to the number you
have already received, hoping that this may meet
the eye of some other sufferer, and that they may
derive the same benefit that I have done. x°. u
are at liberty to make what use you think fit of this
testimonial. —I remain, yours gratefully, Charles
E. Howard, Royal Hot n l. Morpeth, N.S.W.

The six families rule Westralia :
In the House of Assembly, of 29 members, some 23 are
either blood-relations or closely connected by inter-
marriage the Legislative Council of eight (including ex-
officio) members being similarly constituted.
Since 1878, the consumption of spirits in
Maoriland has decreased 48 per cent., of imported
ale 60 per cent., and of ale locally manufactured,
22 per cent. Returning prosperity and “ labour
legislation ” are making Maoriland sober.

to—Roman Catholics and Orangemen ; licensed
victuallers and the teetotal party ; the banks and
their creditors; the Protectionists and |the Free-
traders.
The canny J. A. MTherson has been
elected Chairman (virtual leader) of S.A. Labour
M’s.P. He was chief organiser of the party in its
two big election triumphs, and up to date has
been proof against political temptation for the
sake of his party.

Politics in Victoria, for the last 15 months,
have consisted of little but Matthew Davies.
Governments stand and fall by one blessed gaudy
attorney, whose only claim to consideration was

To Parkes’ eulogistic references to the
British constitution are now invariably attached
commendatory allusions to the constitution of the
United States. That is a characteristic developed
only within the last few years—with the growth
of Australian Republican sentiment.

Salisbury has stated to an Ulster meeting
of Orangeman that he didn’t know, and never
spoke to, the deceased Parnell. Which, when you
turn it the other way about, only amounts to the
equally interesting fact that Parnell never spoke
to Salisbury. Always stand a fact on its head.
“Hume Black, special English bagman for
the Q. Government Meat Export Co., has received
numerous enquiries regarding the conditions of
settlement on the land in Queensland.” This is
diplomatic slang for saying that the great Par-
liamentary land-grab-railway syndicate is smelling
around.
The Maoriland surplus of £511,000 is a
splendid monument to John Ballance, and the
Labour Ministry generally. Ballance’s Ministry
was the first Labour Cabinet which ever held
office in Australasia, and it is the only one in
Australasia whose accounts have come out right
for many years past.
That most virile of Jingoes, Iludyard Kip-
ling, raises another whoop :
Fair is our lot—O goodly is our heritage!
(Humble ye, O people, and be fearful in your mirth)
For the Lord our God Most High
He hath made the deep as dry,
He hath smote for us a pathway to the ends of all the
earth !
This is a stave of “ A Song of the English,” the
official yell of the Imperial Institute crowd who
hooted Gladstone all round the building. It will
be seen that the Almighty’s services have been
exclusively secured by the J. Bull syndicate.

The Forrest Government (W.A.), pigtail-
scared by the smallpox epidemic, has issued a
proclamation that no Chinaman shall enter the
province for five years, “except with the permis-
sion of some duly-authorised person. ihis is
nkasing that no Chinaman shall be allowed to land
except a Chinaman who happens to live by
breathing.
Ex-Treasurer Garvan’s absence from
N.S.W. Parliament has saved one more split in the
Protectionist ranks. Acerbities, barely tolerable,
have disappeared with change of air, and he now
listens tranquilly to Ministerial projects which,
seven months ago, he would have fought- _ Garvan
smashed two Governments and is ambitious of
scoring further.
The Supreme Court Bill, which passed
N.S.W. Legislative Council, the other night,
makes, among other changes, trial by jury in civil
cases optional with the litigants. In June, 61
years ago, Mr. Bulrner presented a petition to the
Imperial Parliament from N.S.W. residents pray-
ing for trial by jury in all cases, civil and criminal.
Times change and change.
The “ Queensland United Licensed
Victuallers’ Association” circularised its mem-
bers to vote for MTlwraithians because the Oppo-
sition might re-impose an excise duty on beer,
while “ the published manifestoes of the Labour-
party are, in some respects, antagonistic to the
trade interests of Licensed Victuallers.” Bung
has no love for sober workmen.
Touching the coming election for North
Melbourne, caused by honest Wyllie’s decease,
the Argus remarks that “ the chances of a veteran
such as Mr. Laurens are not to be ignored.”
Alas ! no. Dreary old Laurens used to drivel for
the constituency in days of yore, and Smellbourne
has a weakness for old members because they seem
to have done so much for the country.
Had the Mitchell (Q.) workmen electors
acted on The Bulletin’s advice, and “criss-
crossed ” their contingent votes between Fitz-
gerald and Campbell, a labour-member would now
represent that district in Parliament. Judging by
completed events, Campbell’s chief object was to
“down Fitzgerald” at all hazards. Labour is
always biting off its nose to spite its face.
The excuse to be given to Downing-street
for not receiving another Governor in S. A., after
Kintore’s departure, is that the mansion of the
Deputy-Lord’s Anointed wants an expensive
overhauling. Some enthusiastic Democrats are
willing to bet that, should Chief Justice Sammy
Way receive the appointment, no foreign magnate
will ever reign in Adelaide afterwards. So mote
it be !

SQUATTER MAC ( although strictly religious tries to make his hands work
Sunday as well as the rest of the week) : “Don’t you think it
would be a good day for burning round the paddock, Tim?
Better nor laying about, man.”
TIM : “To-day’s Sunday.”
SQUATTER MAC : “Well; the bible says if your ass fall down the hole on
the Sabbath, you’re to pull it out.”
TIM: “Yes; but it don’t tell yer to go round all the blessed week to find
a hole to put the ass in on Sunday, though.”

Peacock, M.L.A., was said to have pro-
voked a letter from the Vic. Crown Law Depart-
ment requesting him to explain what he meant by
saying something disrespectful about Davies’
“soft” prosecution. Peacock, at latest dates,
had received no such letter, and was asking for it.
Perhaps the letter was written but not issued, as
was the case with those celebrated writs for
criminal libel that never got served upon Simpson.
Dear Bulletin, —At a Q. station which
was a polling-place there were 41 votes polled—
-28 for a squatter. The station storekeeper was
presiding-otticer ; the station manager (the store-
keeper’s boss) was scrutineer for the squatter can-
didate. The numbered corners of the ballot-papers
were not gummed down, but only fastened with
patent paper-fasteners. Do you mean to tell me
that that station manager doesn’t know how every
local elector voted ?— Scribbler.
The Q. law as to Chinese immigration is
that a steamer can only land a maximum number
of Chinamen equal to one per every 500 tons of
tonnage. Well —a China steamer leaves (say)
seven Chinamen at Batavia, to be brought on by
(say) a 8.1. boat; further, it tranships (without
landing) several more to Australian coasting
steamers; and, last of all, it lands its own per-
mitted quota of Chow. How is that for driving a
steamboat through an Act of Parliament ?
“ Should he be arrested, a police-officer
will leave for Colombo by the next steamer to
bring him back to Melbourne.” They couldn’t
dream of sending the police officer away imme-
diately, armed with the same warrant that missed
tire at Adelaide, with a view to saving time, “ in
the event of a true bill being found against ex-
Speaker Davies.” Thus early, The Bulletin
prophesies that Matthew will not be arrested at
Colombo. There is a twinkle in the Fat Man’s
eye which means a good deal.
Parliamentary institutions must be playeu
out, and the Revolution at hand, considering tha
callous indifference with which the English Par-
liament treats an affair like the Hull strike. The
House of Commons reminds one of the Laputans
or Strudbugs, requiring to have their long ears
flapped by attendants who call attention to other
fellows kissing their wives. Take all history, and
you will see this is the unvarying phenomenon—
as with the French Constituent Assembly, at the
outbreak of the Revolution.
How Dangar and Trickett resented Cupid
Smith’s congratulations in N.S.W. Council over
the final passage of the Electoral Bill ! “ This
splendid measure, which my hon. friends strove
so hard to render perfect.” Greasy
Dangar and Trickett’s efforts to “ render perfect”
the Bill simply consisted in trying to wreck the
one-man-one-vote principle, and their ultimate
cave-in was the price they paid for exemption
from the income-tax. Bah ! It was like con-
gratulating a man on the loss of his right hand.
Just now it is interesting to remember
that Duffy was Attorney-General for Victoria in
the early days of the Shiels Ministry. It is more
interesting to remember that Duffy, having come
to verbal blows with Judge Hodges, over the
latter’s light sentencing of G. N. Taylor (who was
subsequently tried again on a larger charge and
sentenced some more), was pushed out of the
Shiels Ministry and promised the Speakership,
which ultimately fell to Thomas Bent, Esq. The
question arises—would ten banks have closed
their doors if Duffy had remained Attorney-
General all through the past year? Also, why
was the gentleman who disapproved of light sen-
tences shunted just as Matthew Davies began to
look like a troublesome matter ?
Dear Bulletin, The six-months-resi-
dence franchise qualification is the chief engine
by means of which the Queensland capitalist de-
frauded Labour out of its vote at the late election.
On the Australian Pastoral Co.’s stations in the
Maranoa district, men were discharged in large
numbers a few weeks before the election, not-
withstanding the certainty that their services
would be required in a short while for lamb-cut-
ting and mustering. They were “sacked,” appar-
ently, only to make them “travel” and thus
“disfranchise” themselves.” The majority of
bush-hands in Queensland are disfranchised slaves
and must remain so until every sane male adult is
permitted to vote, irrespective of what part of
the country he may happen to reside in.—Yours,
G.L.
The diary of Townshend, the crank who
attempted to murder Gladstone in the cause of
Unionism, is a beastly disagreeable adyt. for the
great Tory party. Tins pleasant maniac kept a
record of how he was gradually worked up to
homicidal frenzy, the said record consisting chiefly
of extracts from the speeches of Salisbury, Bal-
four, Saunderson, Parson Kane, and others, urging
the Ulster men to armed “ resistance,” bloodshed,
and civil war. After each extract followed a
moral reflection by Townshend himself, to the
effect that murder'couldn’t be wrong when advo-
cated by such authorities. The last extract
evidently settled the question. It was copied out
carefully, and following it came a remark that
the writer felt quite satisfied now, and was going
to get his gun.

by the meek and lowly preacher who runs that
organ at Glassey, the democrat
A low-minded, narrow-minded, ill-minded scheming

Dear Bulletin, —You do not yet seem to
have fully fathomed the depths of duplicity to
which the “ fat ” men and their journals are pre*
paved to descend in order to injure the first
Government of Maoriland elected on the “one-
man-one-vote ” principle. Were Ananias to
resurrect and visit Maoriland, he would be con-
sidered a veritable George Washington in com-
parison to the “ specials ” of the Conservative
journals. There is no dissension in our Cabinet,
there has never been any, and there is not likely
to be any ; there was no scramble for the vacant
leadership when John Ballance died ; and the
Ministry is not at all likely to go back on anything
their lamented chief held dear. Mr. Ballance’s
particular friend, Robert Stout (another friend of
the people, although he was mistaken enough to
accept a title), has not come forward for the
Inangahua seat to oust “ Dick ” Seddon from the
Premiership, but because he was asked by his
dying friend to do his utmost for the party and
the people. Stout is sure to lose business and
money by re-entering politics, and the canards
circulated by the paid tools of the “fat” men
arise from the utter incapability of their masters
to understand a man doing anything for love.
Otherwise your leader of May 6 was accurate. —I
am, &c., One Who Knows.
The use of cocaine, as an alternative to
nitrous-oxide gas, in dental operations, is daily
practised by J. I. Marshall, dental surgeon. King-
street Arcade (George-st. entrance), Sydney. Mr.
Marshall has gained .reputation as the author of a
popular treatise on “ The Teeth.”

There are pleasant times ahead for the
“New Australians,” who start this month. They
are going in a small, wooden sailing vessel, and
will reach Cape Horn about the coldest and most
horrible season of the year, when the sun rises at
half-past nine and sets at half-past two, and there
are nineteen hours of darkness. The winds are
even more uncertain off the Horn than they are in
most other places, and a sailing ship may beat
about for four weeks or more, before she struggles
round that stormy headland. It is a joyous ex-
perience, weltering along in that pale-green sea,
with the crew incessantly shovelling the snow off
the deck, and scraping the ice off the rigging, and
wrestling with the frozen sails, while all the
fiends of the frozen zone howl in the blast over-
head, and the huge green waves which roll
round the world without a break in all the 360
degrees of longitude incessantly rush after the
frozen ship. The temperature is some unspeak-
able depth below freezing - point, and dreary
greenish or bluish or dead-white icebergs wallow
along in the troubled waters, and a pallid, watery
sun hangs like the bald head of a devil in the
watery sky. The man who has once gone round
the Horn per sailing ship in mid-winter never
desires to go there again. It is better to get out
at Valparaiso and walk across.

When the Austrian Archduke was in
Sydney the other day he wanted a dog—a fox-
terrier, you understand —and money was no object
so long as he got a first-class animal. Several
dogs were submitted, but he wouldn’t look at
them—they were not class enough. At last a
message was sent to a gentleman known as a
breeder of the very best fox-terriers in Sydney.
He sent three dogs for the Archduke’s inspection,
the prices ranging from 20 to 30 guineas, and an
old servant of the owner took the dogs for the
Royal potentate’s inspection. His Highnessgreatly
fancied one dog, and, after jabbering away in
Czach or Croat or something, he made an offer
which was translated to the old man as follows :
“The Archduke much likes this dog and will
give £3 for him.” This fairly staggered the old
fellow. “ Three pounds for that dog?” he said —
“ you ought to buy dogs, you ought! Why , we
loouldn’t take £S for that dog’s tail." They didn’t
deal.
Why do so many
“respectable” women
always steal when
they think they have
a chance ? The other
day, a gorgeous
dame, arrayed in
purple and fine linen,
with leg -of - mutton
sleeves of the fullest
dimensions, drove up
in her carriage to a
fashionable Sydney
doctor’s. She was
shown into the wait-
ing-room, which hap-
pened to be un-
tenanted ; on the
table was a terra-
o ilta bowl of water-
lily bulbs just sprout-
ing. On the maid
returning, after an-
nouncing the visitor,
she surprised her
wrapping her hand-
kerchief round one
of the wet bulbs
(value, about six-
pence) and conveying
it to her pocket. The
girl was Scotch, and,
with the thoughtful-
ness of her nation,
merely remarked;
“Better put it back,
ma’am, it will die out
of water ! ” The lady
was so disgusted at
the impertinence of
the maid that she did
not wait to see the
doctor.
Another case in
paint happened on the
Sydney suburban line
last week. A lady
got out and left her
umbrella on the seat.
At next station a
well - dressed woman
was observed to walk
up the aisle, when,
at the seat whereon
the forgotten utn-
brella lay, she dropped her own on top of it, then
picked up the two and inarched off.
The “ Australian soldiers” in England are
being boomed by cablegram until they are a weari-
ness to the soul. Almost every day a fresh item
comes along setting forth that they have met
somebody, or been noticed by somebody, or some-
body has been graciously pleased to recognise
them in some way or other. For instance ;
The Queen has commanded the presence of Major F. C.
Hughes and Captain Dodds to an evening-party at
Buckingham Palace, on Monday next.
General Lord Roberts, late Commander-in-Chief of
India, invited Corporal D. D. Mackinnon, a member of the
Victorian Horse Artillery team, into the Royal box. He
shook hands with him.
Just think of it. He shook hands with him!
Their flesh oame into contact —the flesh of a lord
and a plain Australian corporal!! There was a
joining together of cuticle !!! Mackinnon felt —he
actually felt—Roberts, and, what wasmore, Roberts
felt him! !!! And it is so rarely that Roberts
or any other lord does feel an Australian that the
fact is well worth cabling 16,000 miles. The
Australian teams will come back, after a little
more of this sort of thing, in a beastly state of
“ loyalty.” That result is absolutely inevitable.
’Scene : Occasion of Governor Duff’s land-
ing at Sydney. Two women, each carrying a
baby, struggling to get down to Prince’s Stairs in
time. The fatter of the two sees a big crowd
hanging over the rails at the old Manly Wharf,
stops and wedges in amongst the crowd. Two
minutes pass, other female gets impatient and
calls out, “ ’Ere, I say, ’Lizer, ain’t yer cornin’ ;
we’ll be too late for the bloomin’show.” Other
female, in impressive tones, “Oh, rot’im ! Let
’im go. Dash “yer old Guvernor, I’m agoin’ ter
stay ’ere and watch these coves* a-fishin’ for the
dead baby.”

Even the Imperialists must admit the
gradual wane in the enthusiasm with which
British Governors are received in Australia. The
machinery for working up a furore over the advent
in Sydney of Gov. Duff was a dead failure. Mere
curiosity, not monarchical devotion, obviously
ruled the crowd. “Hats off!” shouted excited
officials along the line as the carriage approached,
and the peremptory order raised many a laugh,
but nary a hat.

A Melbourne bookmaker che other day
sued for divorce from his wife on the ground of
her alleged adultery with one “De Vere.” The
evidence disclosed that the real name of “De
Vere ” was Gilligan. Now it is a great social fact
that the De Veres are usually Gilligans in dis-
guise.
« • #
A Southern legal dignitary happened
recently to find the siren of his bijou villa enter-
taining, as is understood to be usual in such eases,
his trusted friend. The row which followed
brought the police in, who, in the end, were the
only parties who profited by the transaction —to
the extent of some £7O. Of such are the terrace of
houses and the gold watch and chain.
Dear Bulletin, —Concerning your remark
that the threatened invasion of female compositors
would best be met by admitting them to male-
union membership. This would, I fear, lead to
wholesale reduction of wages. Women seem never
happier than when working at some “genteel”
employment for next to nil. A case in point.
The charge in Sydney for writing 3-line addresses
on envelopes (for circulars, etc.) was for long ss.
per 1000, and little enough, too. A firm of women
has now reduced this to 35., a rate at which body
and soul must only be but loosely held together.
And yet one rarely meets with a woman who can
cook even a chop properly, and domestic servants
are at the usual premium.—H.V. A.

Every Chinaman trades under an alias.
The Chin Ling of the vegetable truck is Hop Fat
in private life, and so forth. In Australia it is
unusual for the Chow to go to prison under his
ancestral name, and his sworn evidence mostly
begins with a lie.

A sweet, shy little thing, under 17, the
chief exhibit in a late Rockhampton (Q.) abduc-
tion case, not only denied that she was abducted,
but swore that she abducted the man. She des-
cribed feelingly how she rounded up and saddled
the horses, leading by a halter the moke Adonis
rode. Further, she told the court that prisoner
had informed her that he was a defenceless married
man, but a trifle like that wasn’t going to prevent
her rescuing him from domestic slavery.
• * *
An old woman of 70 years, poor and
friendless, arraigned before a Sydney bench for
thieving one day last week, pleaded kleptomania.
The court could be heard laughing on the other
side of the street, as it sent her away for two
months.

boats packed fpll of English beauty going out to

BEHIND THE BAR-A DESECRATION OF TENNYSON.
( FOR THE BULLETIN. )
Gray eyes and gamboge hair !
One barmaid of “ The Crown ” !
Ah, will that beaming siren still be there
When I go next to town ?
When over-night much spirit I had quaffed.
How I was wont to bless
That nymph who, smiling, mixed my morning draught
Of B. and S. !
That holiday has gone !
Now wintry breezes blow
In fitful gusts about my hut upon
The Warrego.
Hard times foretell that for a “ down-South ’’ spree
The day is distant far ;
And I no more, in Sydney town, may see
That girl behind the bar.
THE BREAKER
Hee-hee ! Hee-hee ! the wind blows free,
Sir Matthew rides the rol-ling sea;
A change of air he means to seek,
No sign of health was his blo-o-oming cheek.
But, ere he sailed from Austral’s shore, the knight
Drank just one toast to those who “ made it
right ”
Here’s a health to the Salier !
Here’s to the German gent
Who would not wait until —too late —
A “tee.” was sent.
Sailing, sailing, over the briny foam,
I’ve done with stormy meetings, and
I’ll end my days at “ home ”
Sailing, sailing, far from my old friend, Bent;
They gave me a show
To pack, and go—
And, Heaven be praised ! I went.
The shearing’s clean and deep,
Each snip than last is nearer,
The wool is bound to fall.
The shearer shears the sheep,
The squatter shears the shearer,
The parson shears ’em all.
LOVE AND MARRIAGE.
Young Love is the garden of magical birth
Which the genii planted, .
And Marriage—the gate which conducts us
earth ''
From the region enchanted.
P.L.
We sang, not very long ago,
“ The world is all a fleeting sh6w
For man’s illusion given.”
Let’s add —the best seats in the show
Are taken up where’er you go,
And, if the truth some people know,
It’s much the same in Heaven.
Queensland.
P. LUFTIG.

which are in the hands of men of stability and
repute, who court all possible inquiries, will bo
found further detailed in our business columns.*

* * •
A Christchurch (M.L.)gentleman who had
ideas about bleaching clothes by electricity, lately
fixed up a wire for a clothes-line and connected it
with a strong battery. He was called out after
dark by fearful howls in the garden, and going to
the spot he found the lady who lived next door
writhing in agony and vainly trying to let go a
wet table-cloth and a garment which she had
grasped, one in each hand. She hadn’t meant to
steal them, but she had been overcome by the
yearning to find out whether the other woman’s
table-cloth was as fine as her own and whether she
had real lace on her night-dress, and she had leant
across the fence to feel these two articles. Then,
in some horrible and unexplained manner, they
seemed to seize her and drag her bodily over the
palings, and after that she had a vague idea that
she was hanging by the hands and someone was
beating her with a red-hot poker. Before the
current could be turned off the articles tore in two
and let her down with a whack on the garden-
path, and then, feeling that the situation was too
difficult of explanation, she gathered up her skirts
and ran for it. But next day she apologised by
letter, and peace was nominally restored.

Dear Bulletin,—Don W. W. Head—
the partner of Don Billy Lane—of the New
Australia expedition, assures the Sydney
Telegraph that the intending emigrants
are noc gathered in barracks at Balmain
but are mostly “staying with friends” in
Sydney. If there isn’t a “ barrack ” at Balmain,
then what does Don W. W. call that establishment
where about 70 Paraguayans pig together, and
where all the old newspapers and orange-peel in
creation seem to be chucked over the fence, and
where the right-of-way is never cleaned because
the argonauts are too busy dreaming about the
Paradise that is to come, and where the children
of the expedition invade the next man’s garden
and blaspheme volubly when they are told to quit ?
If the New Australians are staying with friends
there are altogether too many of them staying
with one friend, and they don’t seem to have
picked out a very clean friend either.—B.B.
« • •
Even the worm will turn. Even a saint
of God may be provoked to retaliation. A certain
Rev. Father in a Sydney suburb, before proceed-
ing to read out the late “ Easter Dues ” list, made
some severe remarks about the meagreness thereof,
lie then proceeded to reel off the list of donors.
The three to five-pounders got the places of
honour. Then: “The followin’ gev’wan pound
each;” and there came a row of names. “The
followin’ gev’ ten shillin’s each,” and more names.
Here the holy man, fixing a calm, steely glare on
the front seats, observed: “The followin’ </ev :
nuthin’ each.” At this stage, several prominent
citizens suddenly began to squirm in their seats.
One whitewaistcoated potentate was too much
affected to move, and was observed to fan him-
self flabbily with a handkerchief until he finally
collapsed, and was dragged out in a broken-up
condition.

« * *
The Earl of Longford, we read, was lately
summoned for knocking off the tile of a disloyal
newspaper man who refused to uncover to the
tune of “ God Save the Queen.” The charge was
proved; but the case was dismissed, and it is
understood that the haughty British peer has a
divine right to “ stouch ” the nail-keg of any per-
son who refuses to salaam to his discordant fetish.
The writer is here reminded of an incident he
witnessed in a Castlemaine (Vic.) bar during the
recent Patterson-Longmore mill. A supporter of
Longmore’s was holding forth on politics to an
irrigating crowd, and presently relapsed into
expressions of vitriolic contempt for the Queen,
Albert Edward, and all the royal family. “ Why,
hang it, man !” exclaimed one moistened elector,
“ you are the very fellow who fired me out of
Merton’s pub. at ' Eaglehawk three years ago
’cause I wouldn’t drink the Queen’s health with
you !” “So I am,” replied the orator, regretfully,
“and do you know I’ve been looking for you for
over two years to ask you to kick me? Kick me
hard, please.”

“ It was aisy to live with Murphy when he
was drunk,” said a woman, giving evidence lately
in a wife-beating case at Ashburton (M.L.) “He
was always good then, but when he was sober he
was a baste that no one could live with.” Teeto-
tallers are hereby invited to consider the case of
Murphy and many like him. There are men who
require to be kept tight at the public expense, and
who should be arrested and sent to gaol whenever
found sober or insufficiently drunk.

(Melb.) military reviews to rail or rope off reserves

enclosures are always two in number, and are
designated the “M” and “N” respectively.
The method of drafting in vogue has caused the
“M” to he expanded to “Mashers” and the
“N” to “Nobodies.” Meanwhile, the People
breathe the pure air of the open park,

Dear Bulletin,
—Yes, the Tasma-
nian “ half-castes ”
(thepureTasmanian
aborigines, as you
know, are utterly
extinct, but their
blood runs in the
veins of the mixed-
bloods of whom I
am writing) do eat
much mutton-bird,
the season for which
is just over. Mut-
ton-bird is a dietary
physic, and Tas-
manian half-castes
are an intelligent
race. Nearly every
man of them is able
to build a boat, and,
as boat-sailors, they
are unsurpassed.
On Cape Barren Is-
land (tire township)
they have a nice
school—daily atten-
dance, about 30;
also a church, built
by themselves, un-
der the superinten-
dence of E. Stevens,
schoolmaster and
amateur parson.
Bishop Montgom-
ery provided the
material. The May-
nard family (li ving
on Flinders) con-
sists of really
fine fellows big
men, with hand-
some features and
flowing beards. The
average half-caste is
decidedly “dicky on
his pins,” and was
“ born tired.” He
plays the accordeon
well, and is fond of
step-dancing to the
accompaniment of a
“squarie ” (bottle of
gin), music, pipe
and tobacco; nothing in the world can trouble
him while it lasts. One half-caste lady has a
beard that many a man would be mightily proud
of. But the half-caste girls are mostly good-
looking.—Yours, &c., Mutton-Bird. Flinders
Island, Bass’ Straits.

The Happy Yalley (S.A.) waterworks, now
being constructed, are on the site of a closed
cemetery. The S.A. Government has been com-
pelled to disturb hundreds of corpses, and rebury
them. What the public now wants to know is,
supposing the Government should overlook a de-
ceased, will the aforesaid public have to drink him
along with the water?

The other Sunday a sensation was caused
on Port Melbourne pier by an elderly gentle-
man prancing in the midst of a crowd. Some
thought it a case of sudden lunacy, and others that
he was performing for halfpence. It turned out
that the man was an Argus reporter, up one of the
legs of whose pants a rat had taken refuge.

“Looking Backward ”Being Tried.
Editor Bulletin,— lt may interest your readers
to know that preliminary steps are being taken in
this part of the world to demonstrate that
“Looking Backward” is capable of practical
interpretation. Land has been obtained maniple
area, and it is proposed to build, h/l co-opcvatnc
labour, the Mexican Western Railway, in icturn
for further concessions. ,
An idea of the whole scheme: The Credit
Fonoier C 0.,” numbering some 60,006 members,
has been in existence for some years, and claims
that it is not a follower of Bellamy, but that
Bellamy got the whole of the main ideas in his
novel from the Co.’s literature ! President, A.
K. Owen ; office, Room, 723, No. 32 Nassau-street,
New York, U.S.A. . .
At present, there is only actual room in the
forefront for hardy pioneers, able and willing to
face and stand pioneer hardships. In any case,
no new member is received without a permit
granted by Owen, and those coming without will
find themselves stranded in a foreign land, where
a foreign tongue prevails, amid hostile Indians,
and a long way from any where.
Topolobarnpo Bay is in the Gulf of California,
Mexico. There are two harbours, an outer and an
inner. On the bar of the outer harbour at low
tide, there is a depth of 25ft. When the pro-
jected railway is finished, the Australian
and China mails, if landed at lopolo-
bampo, will be transportable to New York
in three or four days less time than by the
present route, Topolobarnpo being that much
nearer to all the Eastern and Southern cities of
America than is ’Frisco, in the line of route under
consideration.
The inner harbour is 40 miles long and from
7 to 10 wide, and is possibly unequ tiled in the
world for yachting, &o. The scenery is fine, in
my opinion, Topolobarnpo possesses as picturesque
a panorama as that seen from the summit of
Mt. Kden, Auckland, Maoriland —which Bouci-
cault declared to be the finest in the world, ft is
superior as a site for a future model city, which
has been prospectively named “Pacific City •
there being room enough to rival London in size,
and natural advantages sufficient to exceed I ans
in beauty. A magnificent beach frontage improv-
“TO MEMORY DEAR.”
MRS. SWEENEY: “Pace be wid him, poor man. I s’pose ’tis after
missing him a great deal you’ll be, Mrs. Kelly?”
MRS. KELLY: “Bless you, me jewel, but it’s up at th’ pub. below where
he’ll be mished entoirly.”
able cl la promenade park at Havana, a festive
bathing paradise that may eclipse Scarborough.
Climate, clear and bright, neither too hot nor too
cold ; little winter and gentle summer. Land
suitable for semi-tropical and some tropical fruits.
The colonists have already dug an irrigating
canal, erected a Custom House, constructed
several roads, and made numerous surveys. The
new city is plotted, but no permanent residences
have yet been erected. The intention is to keep
ahead of, or at least abreast wdth, all the most
advanced and perfected designs in houses, streets,
means of transport, parks, halls, theatres, &c.
Any suitable scheme of new co-operative life
may be tried here —the “ Looking Backward ”
idea; the “Freeland” scheme, as outlined in
Cowdry’s “A Tramp in Societythe individual-
competitive - co-operative and profit - sharing
schemes of Michael Flurscheim (Ger.); Henry
George’s single-tax proposals—if he or any of his
followers choose to come and try it; &c., &c.
Lands and all public utilities are to be vested in
a central body, corporation, or trustee, the
functions and powers of which are to be defined
by charter, tightly preventing the incursion of
any “syndicate” and impregnable to capitalistic
“cornering ” ; the lands to be so vested that they
cannot be sold, or diverted from the original pur-
pose. This central body can lease land to any
bona-fide body of individuals, and it may build
irrigating canals, transport lines, piers, ware-
houses and public utilities generally, but it must
not engage in the manufacture of any articles of
merchandise, nor in production of any kind ; these
things are to be left to the several schemes, as
each may think best. Individuals may obtain
individual residence lots, or acres for cultivation,
for the terms of their lives and those of their
children, but only to the first generation, after
which the lots revert to the central company. In
short, the land-title is a use for life, or lease.
There are at present some 500 people here, doing
purely pioneer work—clearing brush, making sur-
veys, building roads, and preparing land for culti-
vation. Even these 500 are divided into two
groups—one group recognising individual control
of products, and pooling its produce with its own
agency ; the other working under a Board of
Directors, with departmental overseers. The
former have some 267 acres in process of being
planted, by putting in little farms up to 40 acres.
The latter have cleared and planted 12.) acres,
with 700 orange-trees in orchard and 1000 in
nursery, 300 banana-plants, 1000 grape-vines,
10,000 strawberry-plants, and garden and field
crops. The first efforts, of course, are to make the
colonies self-sustaining, by producing a surplus to
get groceries and outside necessaries. Manufac-
tures will be attempted later, and it is believed
that capital can be borrowed at 3 per cent, for this
purpose.
The project is an attempt to find practical
answers to theoretical social queries. If it fails,
there will be one more tombstone over dead human
endeavours and hopes ; if it succeeds, the millen-
nial dawn is not a chimera, and the co-operative
system will supersede the competitive. If this
letter rouses Australian interest, I shall be glad to
furnish any further details asked for, and we
eagerly look for suggestions and comments from
The Bulletin.— Yours, R. J. Kendall. Topolo
bampo Bay, Sinaloa, Mexico, March 25, 1893.
The Pall Mall Gazette errs in stating
that Livingston Hopkins “took Phil May’s place’ -
on The Bulletin staff. As a matter of fact, Mr.
Hopkins was attached to The Bulletin staff
years before Mr. May came to Australia.
The tone of Melbourne Argus demo
craticises steadily, but the repenting journal con-
tinues to frighten the uneducated with special
articles, reprints of lectures, and “ high-class
matter which only a few care to read. Such
things are really extras to a bulk of general in-
formation which equals the whole supply of news
in the Age. Yet, somehow, the working man
judges the paper by the portions he might avoid
and sums it up as “ dry hash.”
Whoever has need for a plough, or other
agricultural implement, should call on Messrs.
J. & C. Howard, of 280 g Pitt street, Sydney,
where will be found the largest and most varied
stock in the city. Howards’ ploughs, in par-
ticular, include many valuable varieties obtainable
from no other makers. The wrought-iron Colonist
plough, in seven different sizes varying from
1161 bs. to 3721b5. weight, has been in use for many
years, and lias established a better record than
any tiling else of its kind. For deeper work the
firm’s digging plough, which breaks up the soil to
any depth from three to 16 inches, is unrivalled.
The Governor digging-plough is a larger variety
of the same implement with sundry additions and
improvements. It cuts both deep and wide, and
kills and buries everything smaller than a tree
that comes in its way ; also, it gets oyer twice as
much ground as an ordinary plough with no extra
horse - power. The double - furrow Colonist
plough—a machine which lias proved its ability
to demolish even the tussac grass of Maori-
land, an obstruction which is almost as solid
as a cathedral—has a special recommendation, in-
asmuch as it is easily convertible into a double-
furrow digging plough, and thus the buyer has
two machines for the price of one. The “ Simplex”
patent steel harrow is another of Messrs. Howard’s
specialities ; it folds up into a small compass, and
is stronger and lighter than any other in the
market. The firm lias taken 46 first prizes in
England for ploughs, harrows, horse-rakes, hay-
makers, straw-trussers, &c., and has also taken
prizes in all the provinces of Australasia. At
Melbourne Exhibition in 1881 it scooped in no
less than 10 first awards, and in Maoriland alone
has taken enough medals to salt a gold mine. The
firm’s catalogue includes every kind of implement
that can be wanted by the farmer, pastoralist,
susrar-planter, or vigneron, all cheap, durable, and
of the very best construction.
Our Boys.
To see the large crowds anxiously scanning and
minutely criticising, the various telegraphic
advices of the progress of our Australian
cricketers, which are placed outside the various
news agencies, is one of the daily experiences oi
Sydney life, and the interest evoked is not un-
warranted, for “Our Boys ” are, once more, mak-
ing the Old World players, play second fiddle at a
game peculiarly their own. Their skill and stamina
spring from the possession of strong nervous, as
well as physical power, and so long as the Aus-
tralian Eleven continue to brace up their nervous
and muscular systems by the use of Australia’s
premier remed} 7 , “ Marshall’s Tonic Nervine,” so
long will the spectacle be witnessed of delighted
crowds gazing at the records of their wondrous
successes. Concerning “ Marshall’s Tonic Nervine”
Professor Cansdell writes :
“ It gives me great pleasure in recommending to my
athletic friends your well-known ‘Tonic Nervine,' which
is the best strengthening tonic I have ever taken.”
And this eminent athlete’s opinion is verified by
the redoubtable and unconquered Larry Foley,
“ who should know, you know,” for he states :
“I must candidly admit that I have used it for years,
and always found it wonderfully effective when feeling a
little off, and I not only speak from my own experience,
but have heard numbers of the boys (athletes) say that,
for training purposes, they have found nothing equal to
it when taken in a little water twice a day.”

Amusements.
THE EQUITABLE BUILDING SOCIETY,
Brisbane.
THE cessation of BANKING CONVE-
NIENCES throughout the Australian
Colonies, and the subsequent exclusion of in-
tending Subscribers, rendered it impossible to
complete the issue of 103,000 shares by the 20th
May as was confidently believed would have been
done had the usual facilities for transmitting
money existed.
Accordingly, and in response to a strong desire
from all quarters that a complete subscription
should be obtained, the Committee decided to
POSTPONE THE BALLOT and have now
determined to CONTINUE THE ISSUE OF
SHARES, being confident that immediately the
usual Banking facilities are restored, the required
number will be rapidly subscribed, when the list
will be at once closed and THE BALLOT PRO-
CEEDED WITH.
In accordance with the Rules of the Society the
Committee have decided that THE BALLOT
SHALL BE HELD BEFORE THE END OF
THE YEAR, but it is expected that the List will
be fully subscribed at a much earlier date, and the
Committee pledge themselves that no effort will
be spared to expedite the holding of the Ballot.
As previously advertised, UNDER NO CIR-
CUMSTANCES will the FIRST PRIZE (Bank-
ing Chambers, value <£30,000) be withdrawn.
Shareholders are particularly notified that the
whole of the Subscribed Funds were specially
secured against the Titles of the Properties form-
ing the Prizes, and are intact, being in no way
affected by Bank suspensions.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The new rules of the V.R.O. embody English
Jockey Club legislation against jockey proprietorship of
racehorses. Only those riders who train their own
animals are to be allowed to register colours, and sub rosa
purchasing of horses by jockeys will rank as a turf crime
—when found out. However, considering how quickly
secrets “get about” in the racing world, it should be
difficult for the mannikins to keep a stud it the V.R.O.
means real business. As for the rule forbidding jockeys
to bet—well, that will not make much difference.
Jockeys seldom bet in the first person, and the person
they do bet with can’t be compelled to “ split ’ on the
transactions, not by all the stewards on earth. The
proposal to arm stewards with the right to enquire into
“form” at a previous meeting, when it is strikingly
reversed under their august noses, will be welcomed by
the unfortunate public. But the point of this rule, as of
all others, must lie in the application of it. If puerile
“explanations ” are to be swallowed as readily as of yore,
the extension of stewards’ enquiry will produce nothing
more than a little extra ringing of the official bell and
more frequent delays before the “ all right ” flag is jerked
up to the top of the official pole. There is, anyway, a
promising appearance about the new code of racing
regulations for Victoria, although they don’t provide for
the ruthless execution of many turf notabilities who
ought to be hanged in the best interests of sport.
# # *
Likely enough, a good deal of money was won
by fancy wagerers who took odds about actually placing
the first three in the Epsom Derby. The winner was the
“dead bird,” and the two next best favourites followed
him home. But the horses that do this sort of thing don t
belong to Jew bookmakers or syndicates. Oh for a little
more Isinglass in Australian racing !
* » *
Sharp, the one-eyed Surrey bowler, didn’t figure
in last week’s match, and is seemingly a “goner.” The
trip to Australia with Lord Sheffield’s team, in which he
proved himself untrustworthy, settled the prospects of
Mr. Sharp.
• # *
Entries for the V.R.C. Grand National Steeple-
chase are not suggestive of a “good'open race,” and
already it looks like a case of the best of Miller’s starting
a strong favourite. For the G.N. Hurdle, however, there
are several well-known flyers on the list, so if some of
them, such as Pygmalion, have gone in seriously for the
jumping business, the smaller of the two big events should
be interesting.
# * *
The Brush Electric Engineering Co. are trying
to arrange a series of football matches on the Adelaide
Oval under the electric-light. This is awful! God sent
fourteen hours of dark in the winter so that Australia
might have some refuge from the football fiend.
* • *
The great aim of Adelaide cable-manipulators,
so far as the reports of the Australian cricketing tour in
England are concerned, is to boom the local members of
the team. The Advertiskr recently stated that all Lon-
don was excited over George Giflen’s batting, and that
England was paralysed with admiration at the prowess of
Lyons, while another Adelaide paper suggested that the
only reason for the frequent omission of Jarvis from the
playing eleven is that Blackham is jealous of him. It is
recognised that the public want to know about the four
South Australians, and if the man in London omits the
required intelligence, the scribe at this end supplies it.
• • •
Murdoch is- the missing word.
•' * *
The V.R.C. fulminates a rule against jocks
betting, or being part owners, except when trainers. How
about bookmakers owning horses?
* # #
The American papers now have the gall to talk
of “ the fight between an Australian named Hall and
Fitzsimmons, an American.” That is reminiscent of the
fact that Hanlan was a Canadian after Beach bested him.
• * *
On the Hall-Slavin “ match ” the Referee, of
April 23, wrote : “ If Slavin is beaten one may safely say
that all who saw the colonial box in previous matches
must be miserably bad judges of the art. Frankly, I
cannot see where Hall comes in at all, unless Slavin has
gone down-hill at break-neck pace.” The “unless ” came
in at the finish.
# * #
At the V.R.C.’s special meeting, last week, they
wouldn’t listen to any remarks about the cost of enter-
taining vice-royalty. The V.R.C., or any other racing club,
is nothing if not "loyal,” but Lord Hopetoun certainly
ought to give as much as he takes from the show that
rails off a lot of space for him and his party. Perhaps
Hopetoun does make an annual subscription. It so, it
had better be mentioned so as to dispel the prevailing
idea that his Excellency is a white elephant of consider-
able proportions.
Councillor James Garton, of Melbourne, who is
now in very bad health, 30 years ago swam from Sand-
ridge to Wiiliamstown—beating the Hellespont record.
* * *
Coningham annom«ed before leaving by the
Orizaba that he reckone'd’ to net £BOO by the English
tour. Something like £3*2 a week and all expenses paid 1
With Cohen as boss, the shekels are bound to be looked
after. By the way, the latter will come in for a fat share
of the “plunder,” and yet he draws £lO a week from
N.S.W. Lands Office during his leave of absence.
•# ’ *
That story about the poisoning of Slavin,
though probably untrue, is by no means impossible. It is a
notorious fact that Farnan, after beating Jackson, was
dosed in a way that destroyed his constitution and
shortened his days. Not many months ago the writer
saw a certain boxing-instructor enter the ring in a dazed
condition, to fall an easy prey to an inferior man. The
defeated man has since given up fighting in disgust,
because he has got evidence that he w'as dosed on the
occasion by his own second.
# • #
Dan O’Connor’s party are evidently lying low
for a good thing.
# * *
Larry Foley—and he ought to know—reckons
Jim Hall is tar and away a better man than Fitzsimmons.
Theoretically, perhaps, but Fitzsimmons takes care of
himself and Hall doesn’t.
» • •
Dear Bulletin, — Your readers may remember
how, over a year ago, I predicted that Jackson, on meet-
ing Slavin, would win, and “ win easily,” and afterwards
that when Slavin met “ a first-class heavy-weight as much
money may leave Australia as when Hanlan beat
Trickett?” Though doubted at the time, my words have
come true. Yet, I cannot claim any prescience. Anyone
studying Slavin’s career could feel he was never above
second-rate. Though he beat Dooley in 1887, I never saw
such a chopping-block as the unreliable Mick made of
him for the first ten rounds. When Slavin left
Sydney and all were calling him a cur, I had a
good word to say for him, yet could never
understand why Melbourne people made so much of him.
In Australia, Slavin did nothing wonderful. He never
actually beat Burke, as his barrackers say. In their first
contest Burke tried to knock him out in a limited number
of rounds, and didn’t; in their second, though the ring
was smaller, he, much bigger, did not knock out Burke
any more than Sullivan did Mitchell. He beat Costello
at Albury because the Buffalo could not act on grass. On
the boards, at the Alexandra, Martin proved the better
man. Yet Burke and Costello were beaten by middle-
weights like Creedon and Pritchard. The Farnan whom
Slavin beat was a wreck of his former self. 1 never be-
lieved in Slavin’s improvement in England. His association
with Baird and Mitchell could not improve his condition.
His great feats were beating Smith and Kilrain, like him-
self, mere newspaper champions.—Yours, &c., V.
* # #
People anxiously wait to see Cremorne’s weight
for Melb. Cup. After his easy Birthday Cup win he may
be up to anything.

Wanted—A Prophet.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The office of prophet attached to the State
Has no one to fill it,
But where is the gifted one destined by Fate
To collar the billet?
A high monthly payment we offer in coin
To a truthful foreteller,
And unlimited fizz we would let him purloin
From the vice-regal cellar.
The duties are light—just to whisper the name
Of the next big race winner,
Or to tell us in time that the favourite’s lame,
Or his jockey a sinner.
To warn us what mornings we ought to takeout
Our best silken umbrella,
And apprise us when Broken Hill shares are about
To go back on a “feller.”
To give us the tip when our friends will come round
To go through us at euchre—
To disclose, ere it breaks, that the bank is unsound
Where reposeth our lucre.
To advise on investments, how far we should go
On stock or debenture,
To tell us what temper our best girl will show
If on marriage we venture.
To predict what ideas will take with the crowd
At the next big election,
So that we as good voters may all shout aloud
For Freetrade or Protection.
We do not demand, with the prophetic soul,
A too virtuous body,
But our seer must not be, if he essays the role,
The purveyor of shoddy.
He need not, in stature, be six feet in his socks,
Nor in presence be regal,
Nor need he look out from beneath his grey locks
With an eye like an eagle.
He need not be free with his tongue or his pen
As both you and I are,
But on one point he must be unlike other men—
He must not be a liar.
M.
44 The moral writers practise virtue after
death.”
“ He that earneth wages, earneth wages
to put them into a bag with holes.” — Haggai 1.6.
The Indian Empire has grown in size 100
per cent, during Victoria’s reign—writes someone.
Gavan Duffy mentions that, visiting strait-
laced Geneva lately, the home of Sabbatarian
Calvin, he was surprised to find the Parliamentary
election proceeding on Sunday, with the poll
taken in a church.
A British Royal Commission not long ago
reported that the British standing army was
mainly composed of weakly boys, while the
Reserve existed largely on paper only. Some
recent North London prosecutions for fraudulent
enlistment prod need am pie evidence that a favourite
game of militiamen is to enlist over and over
again—at 30s. a head bounty for each enlistment
in different battalions, while thousands of Army
Reserve men are also counted a second and even
third time as militiamen. If all branches were
mustered simultaneously, the British Army would
only number about 40 per cent, of its “paper ”
strength.

“ Morocco Bound” is a sort of Gaiety burlesque, at the
London Shaftesbury, with the ballet in half-calf.
The latest: Antoinette Sterling is rather a ferocious-
looking woman, of the Irving type.
A Melbourne theatrical correspondent to Tub Bulletin :
" Tapley, the new tenor, has a light, sweet, tuneful
voice, marred somewhat by a vibrato suggestive at its
worst of a boozer trying to carry a mould of jelly. It
reminds one of Hatton without the power. He is the
nearest approach to a really fetching tenor the firm has
had for a long time. The new man will not be a masher
or a society favourite, as he is comparatively weak in the
legs; the Fat Man’s wives and daughters liking ’em—
well, otherwise.”
As an object-lesson to the visitor to our shores and the
youth of our cities the production of “Never Too Late to
Mend," at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, ought to be highly
educational. “In a lovely dell at the foot of a mountain,
with tree-ferns, waterfalls, and semi-tropic vegetation,"
are camped a tribe of aborigines, who, we are informed,
are denizens of the spinifex desert. The last time we
were in the spinifex desert that was just the appearance
it presented, only there were also groves of oranges,
bananas and grape vines—and free lunches. When the
niggers all sung out br-r-r-r-ah I we remembered them at
once as old friends—from La Perouse.
A “spiritualistic ” controversy was opportunely started
in the Adelaide Register just as the Steens began their
season.
Madame Antoinette Sterling, when in Adelaide, went
down to Yatala stockade and sang to the spirits in prison,
and the syndicators who engaged her protested against the
breach of her agreement, which binds her only to warble
at their concerts. But the entrepreneurs didn’t lose any-
thing. The ladies and gentlemen in quod couldn’t have
taken tickets, anyhow.
“ Never Too Late to Mend” was the greatest success
known in the east of London. Chas. Reade finally won
his interminable-looking suit against Conquest, who had
to pay thousands of pounds, and yet came out the
pecuniary gainer by a long chalk. In the first case. Reade
was beaten by Conquest, who upheld his right to drama-
tise the novel, until Reade, by an afterthought, showed
that the novel was based on a play he wrote long ago.
called “Gold.”
Gourlay, Walton and Shine did w-ell in Adelaide with
the everlasti g “ Skipped.” In ages to come Johnny
Gourlay will be able to play Obadiah Dingle au nature*,
" hich doesn’t mean without clothes on, but without any
necessity for a sham bald head.
