Dear Bulletin, — I have always understood that Wallace
composed “ Maritana” at New Norfolk (Tas.) You favour
Parramatta, and I daresay a Queenslander would back
Humpybong as the birthplace of the opera. Mel-
bourneites probably pin their faith to Brighton as the
spot where the inspired musician ground out his
melodies, while South Australia would hold up the wave-
washed shores of Glenelg. As for me, I will lay my money
on New Norfolk.— Van Demon.

Dear Bulletin, — -Now and then a communication ap-
pears to the effect that Pattie Browne owes her present
position to tutors—in other words, that she is nothing
better than a parrot. This is probably the outcome of
jealousy. In ’B2 or ’B3 the Lotos Amateur Dramatic Co.
gaie Our Boys in Sydney Academy of Music (now
Tivoli). I was Mary Melrose ; and the Belinda of the cast
was 1 attie—the first part she had ever played. The
unconventional reading she gave, and the talent she dis-
played, without a stage-manager to drum the part into
her head, surprised everyone. The audience insisted on
giving Belinda a special call. That Pattie Browne has
achieved a position and made a success in London, no one
can deny; why grudge the girl the kudos due to her
achievement? She had gained her laurels by energy
application and great talent. Long before Mr. Boucicault
or Mr. Beck came to this country, she made one of her
great successes as Lord Johnnie, in the “ Merry Duchess ”
under the management of Messrs. Williamson, Garner,
and Musgrove.—Yours, Myra Kemble.

row of the stalls to go out. Seizing the chance, Wood
said: Ah ! a case in point: you would not have needed
to leave had you brought two bottles in with you.”

One admires the sang froid of the Australian black-
fellow in George Rignold’s “Never Too Late to Mend ”
No stage-fright, no self-consciousness, not a look at the
audience. It takes the ordinary whitefellow years to for-
get the front of the house.

Cabled that some “ladies of the Gaiety Burlesque Co. ”
along with the unspeakably dreary Lonnen and several
others, raided the office of a Dunedin (M.L.) paper the
other day, and unsuccessfully demanded satisfaction for
certain aspersions on the character of the members of the
00. Five cf the chorus-girls then started to belabour the
editor and printer with horsewhips, but were thrown out
Regaining admission, they completely wrecked the in'
terior of the building. The most gladsome feature in the
story is that the depressing Lonnen got a bad thump on
the right eye. Australia wait* anxiously to know what
the paper said about the characters of the Gaiety ladies fo
rouse them to such fury. It is utterly impossible that it
could have reflected in any way on their morality ; and,
in any case, had ic done so, the knowledge of their own
obvious rectitude would have assuredly led them to treat
the accusations of a blackguard with disdain. After
mature consideration, Tins Bulletin is inclined to think
that the editor-man probably aggravated them on some
abstruse theological point.
Paragraphs appear describing Clement Scott’s wedding
at ’Frisco, and how his fiancee came from London “ under
the escort of his friend Austin Brereton ’’—husband of
Edith Bland. Dangerous system, this. All the girls we
have seen escorted altar-wards by the other fellow, have,
ere they had been a week at sea,'been, as it were, hugger!
by proxy, mugged by deputy, and embraced under power
of attorney.

The drivers employed by a Sydney “ pri-
vate enterprise ” ’busman have gone out on strike.
Summarised, the position is this: The kindly
employer (he must live, you know) wants the men,to
work over twelve hours daily for 30s. a week nett.
The remedy for this brutal' state of things—the
raunicipalisation of public vehicles.
As anticipated, the splitting-up of the
Democratic vote has lost the Labour-party the
N. Melbourne seat in the Victorian Parliament,
the Tory man, Reynolds, polling all his crowd
and coming out on top with 801 ; old drivel-by-
the-hour Laurens, 673 ; Prendergast, 670 ; Willie
Gaunson (as usual), 29.
Parkes addressed a Newcastle (N.S.W.)
meeting, the other evening. His bleached halo
saved him from a bad reception. The audience
cheered his hair. His appearance on the stage
was held in the theatre—was not im-
posing ; he looked as if he had existed somewhere,
a long time ago, and once had a meaning, but
was now an obsolete word in the human dictionary.
The speech, which was full of Parkes in various
aspects, was chiefly remarkable for the entire
absence of promises—there were no P.N.’s on the
future which, next to his hair, form Parkes’s
chief asset.
According to the books of the Registrar of
Shipping, Sydney, on 22nd May bills of sale passed
from the former joint-owners,of the barque Royal
Tar to Walter William Head, one of the promoters
of the “New Australia ” movement to nlant a co-
operative garden in the howling wildernesses of
Paraguay, South America. Walter William
Head purchased the Royal Tar for £1620
on 22nd May, and on 23rd May executed
a mortgage of that vessel for £2520 to “Wil-
liam Lane, gentleman ’’—formerly described,
by the way, as William Lane, journalist.
So far as we know, this much-abused term
“gentleman” has a narrow legal meaning, and,
as a mortgage is a legal document, that narrow
meaning must be here taken to apply. If we
understand aright, the legal meaning of “gentle-
man is a man who is not under the necessity
of earning his living any more, but is provided
for the rest of his life. This does not seam to
coincide with the popular idea of Moses Lane,
who is ever seen, “in the mind’s-eye, Horatio,”
battling with the stubborn wilds of South
America, belting down the giants of the Para-
guayan scrub, drilling terrible tunnels through the
adamantine Andes (or the nearest razorback
ridge), and generally warring with the elements.

When Labour shall be Free!
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
O brother discontented —in the outer darkness
Secure in naught save sorrow, all faint-hearted
in your creed ; .
Believing nothing surely—at the best but vaguely
hoping ;
Basely passive in your bondage— -blindly waiting
to be freed.
To whom, fool, are you looking ? Think you, he
will liberate you, ,
He, your owner, pow’rful in his paltry pride ot
pilfered pelf; ,
Despising you, his servile slave, too much to roar
or hate you,
Do you look to him to free you ? Can you so
delude yourself ?
Or do you seek a saviour in the placid politician
Who reckons men and measures but as counters
in the game ,
Of personal self-inter’st, of ignoble self-ambition .
Who, Judas-like, would sell you to your ever-
lasting shame ?
Will you never learn your lesson ? Shall successive
ages find you
Still battling for your life’s-breath in the grasp
of griping greed ?
In the future, as in past time, shall your selfish
passions blind you
To your duty towards your fellow, to the teach-
ings of your creed ?
Who hath sold you into bondage? Who hath
forged the chain around you ?
Who condemned your helpless children to a
heritage of shame ?
Do you ever, fool, consider, whilst you curse the
fates that hound you ?
Ever strive to solve this problem e’er you seek to
cast the blame ?
Do you hold yourself as guiltless? Say, how
dealt you with your brother
In that day when Labour fought those bitter
battles for her life ?
Did you join the rush for places, caring nothing
that another
Might starve to fill your belly? Were you
traitor in the strife.
Lo, the truth is demonstrated ! Know you now
the dread despoiler?
Awake, and grasp the fact, man, —aye, and
profit by it, too ;
One enemy has Labour— only one —the traitor-
toiler,
Th’ Judas that betrayed her! Say, who was
this Judas? You?
Come, deal no more in abstracts, seeking some
abtruse solution,
The way is clear before you, if you only choose
to see;
When th’ toilers stand united —not in bloody
revolution—
But steadfast in one common cause: then
Labour shall be free.
ERNEST F. SQUIRES.

The Bulletin has more than once referred
to the longest continuous speech on record, deli-
vered long ago in the British Columbian Legisla-
ture. It now clips an account of that marvellous
performance :
The measure before the House would, if passed, have
deprived many settlers of their lands. Member De Cosmos
was in a hopeless minority—he stood alone. The job had
been held back till the eve of the close of the session;
unless Legislation was taken before noon of a given day,
the act of confiscation would fail. The day before the
expiration of the limitation De Cosmos got the floor about
10 a.m., and began a speech against the bill. Its friends
cared little, for they supposed that by one or two o’clock
he would be through, and the bill could be put on its pas-
sage. One o’clock came, and De Cosmos was speaking
still-had not more than entered upon his subject. Two
o’clock—he was saying “in the second place.” Three
o’clock—he produced a fearful bundle of evidence and
insisted on reading it. The majority began to have a sus-
picion of the truth—he was going to speak till next noon
and kill the bill. For a while they made merry over it,
hut, as it came on to dusk, they began to get alarmed.
They tried interruptions, but soon abandoned them, be-
cause each one afforded him a chance to digress and gain
time. They tried to shout him down, but that gave him
a breathing-space, and, finally, they settled down to
watch the combat between strength of will and weakness
of body. They gave him no mercy—no adjournment for
dinner, no chance to do more than wet his lips with
water, no wandering from his subject, no sitting down.
Twilight darkened ; the gas was lit; members slipped out
to dinner in relays, and returned to sleep in squads, but
De Cosmos went on. The Speaker, to whom he was
addressing himself, was alternately dozing, snoring, and
trying to look wide-awake. Day dawned, and the
majority slipped out in squads to wash and breakfast,
and the speaker still held on. It cannot be said that it
was a very logical, eloquent, or sustained speech. There
were digressions in it, repetitions also. But still the
speaker kept on ; and at last noon came to a baffled
majority, livid with rage and impotence, and a single
man who was triumphant, though his voice had sunk to
a husky whisper, his eyes were almost shut and were
bleared and bloodshot, ’ his legs tottered under him, his
blacked lips were cracked and smeared with blood. De
Cosmos had spoken twenty-six hours, and saved the
settlers their lands!
More Australian Testimony.
THE SECRETS OE SUCCESS.
Mr. J. B. Warbrick, or “Joe Warbrick,” as In
is familiarly known, was captain of the New
Zealand Native Football Team (better known as
“The Maoris’’), which visited the Old Country
and gave such a good account of itself during tha
season there of 1888-81). This team firmly believed
that St. Jacobs Oil is one of the secrets of success
in football, and kept itself in prime condition by a
free use of it. The'team was so unanimous in its
verdict that the matter is worthy of special men-
tion. Mr. Warbrick, in conversation with one ol
the representatives of the Chas. A. Vogeler Co.,
I in Sydney, after their return, stated : “ I hava
much pleasure in testifying to the efficacy of St,
Jacobs Oil. I have used it constantly since my
first visit to New South Wales in 1884, having
then injured my knee in our first match ; and dur-
ing our tour in Great Britain we have used noth-
ing else, and the New Zealand Native Football
Team all speak in the highest terms of its
qualities. In fact, so much did wo think of it
that we hardly ever travelled without a case
AN ENDORSEMENT.
Mr. J. R. Scott, the successful proprietor of the
leading hotel in Cisborne, N.Z.—the Masonic
Hotel—and the able manager of the above-men-
tioned New Zealand football team on their tour
through Great Britain, endorses the above by
letter, in which he states : “ I have much pleasure
in testifying to the wonderful properties of St.
Jacobs Oil. While there (Great Britain) I got for
the team’s use nearly a dozen different embroca-
tions, but none seemed to do the men the same
amount of good as St. Jacobs Oil. In fact, we
never considered our baggage complete without a
I case of it.”

The Curse of Smith, Senior
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
He was French master in a big boys’ boarding-
school near Sydney. His business there was to
teach 35 medium-sized boys how to inquire daily
in Paris French :
“ Have you seen the hat of the gardener’s (/) uncle ?”
And how to answer Jesuitioally :
“ I have eaten the father’s meat, and thou hast given
Louise some pens and some paper.”
Then there were 15 or 20 smaller boys who were
expected to be well-grounded in such assurances
as, “My father is good. My mother is good. I
have a good brother, and I have also a good
sister.”
Lastly—and in the superlative degree of worse-
ness—there were the seven boys of the top class
whom Antoine escorted daily through the ways
and wiles of Telemachus.
He hated it. He loathed it all. Day after day
he would leave the school —his Ahn, and his
Grammaire, and his Telemaque beneath his arm
—muttering:
“ I vill go back no more. Nevaire. Zere is an
end to all zing.” But next day lie would be there
again, telling Jones sadly that a father could not
be “bonne,” and explaining pathetically to Smith,
senior that:
“ Viz ze verrb to be,
Ze pass partceeple mosst agree,
In gendaire, nombaire and person, ziz ze sob-
jeck.”
Smith, senior, was a white-haired boy, with a
big head and a small and sweet-looking face. He
had a pretty mouth and a generally angelic ex-
pression. It hjud struck Antoine once or twice
that Smith, senior, always looked more particu-
larly angelic when he was feeling and being most
particularly demonish.
One day Smith, senior, gave the first turn to the
resolution of Antoine's life-wheel.
It was a close, sultry day, and hot beyond
description. And it was half-past two in the
afternoon, an hour when all boys in all schools,
and all masters, too, for that matter, begin to
suspect that the big hand goes as slowly as the
little one, and that the slowness of both is more
than mortal man was meant to endure.
Antoine was about to give the medium boys
dictation.
“ Get your slites,” he said.
“ What for?” said twenty voices eagerly,
“S-s-sh,” said Antoine. “ Wonn moment, and
I veel tell you. Now, are you all ready?”
He cleared his throat, and announced: “Ze
Dictoition Examinoition.”
“ Bully,” said Smith, senior, sotto voce. He was
good at spelling. “Fire away.”
“ I vill read ze Englees firrst and zen ze
Froinch,’ said Antoine. “ Are you ready ? ”
They were ready, so he began : “ Ze two soldaire
were ready. Ze eldaire haf hees gun, and ze
youngaire hees sword. Zey begin to fight; soon
ze youngaire keels ze eldaire, who vas hees oncle.
Zen he deeps hees sword in ze blod of hees skin”—
This struck Smith as curious.
“The blood of what ? ” he said.
“ Ze blod of hees skin,” said Antoine slowly.
“ Blood of his skin? ”
“Ze—blod —of—bees skin.’
“Jove, that’s rum. Show us the book, Tony.”
“No, saire,” said Antoine. “You will be
silent. My plice ’ere ees to teach you. Your
plice ees to listen, and be good boy.”
He went on with his reading. Suddenly he
was interrupted with a great burst of uproarious
laughter from a boy in the back seat. He looked
up. It was Smith, senior. He had a book in his
hands—it was another copy of the book Antoine
was reading from, and he had turned the leaves
and found the place, and tried to discover for
himself that mysterious “blood of his skin.”
“ Ha, ha, ha,” he roared.
“Silence, said Antoine, amazedly.
. But Smith laughed on, and the boy next him

Chapter 11.
Smith, senior, grew up, and became what you
would call a man. He was a trifle longer than of
yore. His hair had turned a good many shades
darker. A moustache hid the mouth that had
been so angel-like, and, to Tony, so demon-like in
his boyhood. He was a law-student now. He
was just beginning to mingle in his conversation
with his sisters little legal catches, such as
“advise,” and “without prejudice,” and several
other bywords of the bar.
Already his mother had seen him a Q.O.
Since the days of Tony he had learnt a good
deal of French, but had forgotten it all ages ago.
Now, he took pleasure and pride in avowing his
ignorance of such a language, which is a little way
young graduates have —I have often noticed it.
One morning—he had been to a ball up the river
the night before—ho came into town an hour
earlier than usual. It was the fault of the boat-
system. He had either to be half-an-hour late, or
a good hour early, and he chose the latter, as the
time for the LL.B. exam, was drawing uncom-
fortably near, and the lecture-market had gone up
in value accordingly.
“While I’m waiting,” he said, “I’ll get a
shave.”
Then he remembered that upstairs in the arcade
he was going through at that very moment there
was a barber that a friend had recommended to
him highly only yesterday.
So up the stairs he ran and along the passages.
It was early, hardly eight o’clock, and there were
few people about.
Presently his eye caught sight of the legend on
the glass of a window. “ Hairdresser, Barber and
Dermatologist,” and a little higher up the pane,
“ Antoine Turan.”
He knew the name at once, but for a moment he
forgot just exactly who Antoine Turan was.
Then—ah !he remembered, of course, and, with a
rush, Tony came back among a crowd of remini-
scences, and Smith went in.
Inside there was a little, brown-faced and black-
haired man, with bright and kind-looking brown
eyes, and the moustache and hair-parting of his
profession. He wore a light suit of black alpaca,
and a fresh white apron was tied around his waist,
but for the rest, saving that his coat-sleeves were
black now and not green as of yore, and that the
great hollows beneath his once sad eyes were now
filled in with goodly brown flesh, he was just the
same old Tony who had years ago met a “lyrikin.”
There was the odour of pomatum around him
indeed, but then, that had been around him since
the days of his long-clothed babyhood.
“Hullo!” said Smith. “By jovel It’s you,
Tony 1”
“Eet ees,” said Tony, beaming. “Ees it a
shive zat you vill require,” eyeing him profession-
ally.
“ Yes,” said Smith, “bub you don’t remember
me?”
“Pardon, monsieur, no. I—pardon. Zereis
so many zat I see. But I know ze face some’ow.”
Smith sat down, and Tony got his things ready.
“I say,” said Smith, “do you remember a kid
at old Martin’s school named Smith ? You taught
him French.”
“ Hees hair vas vat colair-e ? ” said Tony.
“ White,” replied Smith.
“Zat vas Smee, senieur,”said Tony, who had
long ago forgiven, though he had never forgotten
him.”
“ And that’s me, Tony.”
“ Eet ees you, monsieur. Non-sense. Ah ! So.
Yes, eet ees you, Meestaire Smee.”
“Jove, I was surprised to see you, Tony,” said
Smith. “I thought you’d really vanished this
life years ago.”
“Vat? Zat I vas dead. Oh, no. I am just
marry.”
“You are? My compliments to Mrs. Tony,
and good luck to you both,” said Smith.
“1 haf had ze good luck at larst,” said Tonv
“ but eet took long in coming. Eet vas a legace—
legacie, zat got me ’ere. Mon onole he leaved it
to me, and I giff up ze teach for evaire. Ah, ze
teach. I did hite it.”
Then he began to shave.
“Ah,” lie said, “zereis a grate develment in
you now.”
Smith couldn't speak, but he gasped an “Eh 1”
“ Yes, zere ees. Grate develment, vat you call
devel-op-ment,.growf in you.”
Anything funny could always tickle Smith. He
had not put on, with his moustache, the blind-
eyed and deaf-eared imperviousness to jokes that
so many a moustache affects.
As Tony’s voice said “develment” in the old,
old way, the words—“He deeped ins sword in the
blod of hees skin ” came rushing back to him. He
saw it all: Tony, angry and excited, his English
becoming more barbarously broken with his
broken temper; himself, little, white-haired Smith,
in agonies of a merciless laughter; the boys all
more or less ditto, and the reminiscence was too
much. He gave a laugh. His head lurched.
Somehow the razor slipped very badly, and then
—and then—
Smith, senior, lay with white, still face upon
the floor. Blood was on the razor, and on him.
Tony knelt beside him. He had tied his hand-
kerchief, and Smith’s, and several cloths, round
the wounded throat. “He felt Smith’s hands,
and face, and found them cold. He wrung
his hands, and tore his hair, and felt his tears
sting hot and thick upon his cheeks.”
“ Mon Dieu !” he cried. “He ees dead, and I
—l—’tis I haf kill him !”
It was early, and few people were about.
Tony shut his door behind him, and went
hastily downstairs.
There was a little, three-roomed house at Pad-
dington, and she, little, grey-eyed Lisa, she was
there. He thought of her all the time, as he ran
along, bare-headed, and jumped into the nearest
cab. He thought of her all the way home, and
the horse’s hoofs seemed to trot to the tune of
“Lisa, Lisa.”
“Mon Dieu !” he moaned, “ zis is too hard, zis
is too grite. All my life haf I served, if not for
ze, zen nevaire against ze. All my life. But all
zings go against me. Ah, ah. It was alwy Smee,
senieur.”
In ten minutes he was home. Lisa heard him
coming, and, opening the door, was there to meet
him. He drew her in and shut the door, and,
seating her on a chair, knelt on the floor beside
her and put his arms around her.
“Oh, Lisa, Lisa, Lisa !” he moaned.
“ Tony, dearest mine, why do you cry?” she
said, affrightedly.
“ I haf kill a man,” he said,
“Thou, my Tony! Antoine, thou! Ah no.”
She held his head close against her heart, and laid
herlittJe, soft, brown hands upon his face. She
hardly understood, but saw that something dread-
ful had happened.
“It has alwise been Smee, senieur.” said An-
toine, hopelessly.
Lisa knew all about Smith, senior. Had not

Chapter HI.
Oinith senior, was a barrister, full-fledged, with
chambers, wig, and gown and a couple of red-
laned brieflets upon his table.
Re had a queer way of speaking. He had had
an accident a few years back. The man who had
all unwittingly been the cause of it was tending
grape-vines for a master at the foot of far-off
French mountains. #
Smith was sitting on his table talking to some
fr '“ Well,” he said, trying to be philosophic despite
■he fifty-guinea briefs that would not come,
< u .r a „ s probates are not much to live for, but I
know one poor chap, who’s doocid glad I’m alive.”
lt was true. Smith needn’t have felt apolo-
getic for the statement. But the others looked
doubtful. They had not yet aspired to chambers,
and encountered some little difficulty with the
Tenth Commandment. So they were dubious,
iust a little, but politely, for Smith’s chambers
were good recreation grounds at lunch times.

Bulcock, a Brisbane (Q.) temperance and

Writes the ‘ National :
papers are Ihe best to advertise in. An advertisement
which is not nad docs no srood, and most daily papers are
only half read before they make way for their successors.
The paper that is read from end to end,” like The Bulle-
tin, “is the sheet-anchor of profitable advertising,” A
good, original weekly, not a hideous jumble of reprinted
stale news spiced with observations about horse-colic and
turnip-diseases, is the best medium. Every man knows
that he skims over a daily, at breakfast or on a tram-car.
He never reads it through. But his favourite weekly is
mentally laid aside for an hour of leisure, and then he
reads it, and reads it right through. The Bulletin cit-
culatcs everywhere in Australasia, and even sells 300
copies weekly in South Africa. Let the business man
personally observe, or ask his traveller or his travelling
friend to keep his eyes open and say which naner is the
most widely-circulated in this continent. There can be
but one answer—The Bulletin.
Emperor of Asia.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
According to the cable the Czar of Russia intends to
'adopt for himself the title of Emperor of Asia :
There is meaning in the music, in the wild, un-
couth refrain
Of the strong North’s rolling anthem in the hymn
of Tamerlane.
Have ye heard it, old-world leaders, as they roar
it from afar?
“Stand aside, the Slav is coming; long live
Russia and the Czar !”
From the bleak, bare hills of Asia, from the forest
of the fir,
They are marching for their Mother, they are
dying fast for her.
Have ye seen her, old-world prophets ? she is fresh
and fair and young,
And around the Tartar’s camp fire songs like this
are loudly sung :
“Men must die, and who for glory falls before the
foeman’s steel,
Half the sting of death escapeth, half her pain
will never feel;
Ours the world will be for taking, ours the gates
already are.
Stand aside! the Slav is coming; on for Russia
and the Czar!”
On the steppes of war-worn Asia, by the frozen
Arctic shore,
Tramp the legions of the Tartar, swelling hourly
more and more.
’Tis the same old tale repeated—Masters, set your
minds at ease.
Was not Rome, the aged, conquered by a people
such as these ?
Did the oW-blood turn to water in the dusk of
ages gone,
When the new- blood surged and thundered round
the gates of Babylon ?
Did the Persian melt before him when the tur-
baned heathen came
With his prophet and his Allah and the scimitar
and flame?
Do ye know it, old-world students, that they
marry to beget —
That the vice we dare not mention has not reached
the Tartar yet ?
Have ye noticed, old-world sages, how this new-
blood, strong as wine,
Surges like the mountain torrent ’cross the
Danube and the Rhine ?
Nations rise, and empires flourish, flourish grandly
to a fall,
And the victor lives to vanish, so ’tis written—
that is all !
Masters, are your cannon ready ? you may need
them very soon,
When the world is set a-dancing to some merry
Tartar tune.
Ivan, keep your rifle loaded and remember what
you are;
“ Clear the way, the Slav is coming—on for Russia
and the Czar J ”

A few definitions from London Truth :
Man.—A biped with prejudices—which he calls principles.
Woman.—A biped with more prejudices—and less prin-
ciples.
A-Perfect Lady.—The hiirhest praise which the scullery-
inaid can accord to her mistress.
A Smart Little Woman. —A young married woman in
search o( a husband—somebody else’s husband.
Marriage—An investment for woman and a speculation
for man.
Friendship.—A game for two, at which only one wins.
Society.—A hotch-potch of pretentious people having
nothing in common but uncommon assurance.
Respectability.—Consistent conformity to inconsistent
mediocrity.
Principle.—Any opinion which it is our individual interest
to support.
Reputation. —More capital with which to deceive our
neighbours.
The World.—A place in which we all hate each other fora
time, in expectation of loving each other later for
eternity.
A Good Chap.—Anyone who might, could, would, or
should lend us money.
A Bad Chap.—The same individual after he has lent us
money.
A Smart Man.—One who affects to despise all but those
who despise him.
A Wealthy Widow.—A woman with a bonus.
A Politician.—One who manipulates the principles of
others for his own interests.
Club.—A man’s refuge from home.
Gun.—An instrument which kills before and which kicks
behind.
Criticism.—The judgment passed by mediocrities upon
their superiors.
Jealous Hatred.—The spontaneous tribute which small
minds pay to great ones.
Contempt.—A sentiment which we all express for each
other, and which we most of us feel for ourselves.
Joe Beef’s Canteen, at Montreal, Canada,
is a weird memory of the past. The Salvation
Army reports that the place was recently “cap-
tured,” and will be fitted up as a food and shelter
dep6t—which, by the way, it,ever has been. It
is not stated that the Army captured Joe Beef
personally, but the “ despatch ” leads one to sup-
pose that Joseph has either died or retired from
business. Anyway, the Canteen, as the present
writer knew it ten years ago, will nevermore shelter
a buffalo in the “loose” box adjoining the great
bar room, nor reverberate with the roaring of
grizzly bears, from their cages and hiding-places
about the premises. The famous skeleton of Joe
Beef’s father, that used to stand in tattered regi-
mentals, with one bony finger on the bar, grinning
an eternal welcome upon frowsy lodgers and dis-
tinguished visitors alike {we were a distinguished
visitor), has gone for good. In the old times, one
was expected to shout a drink for the skeleton, as
a matter of courtesy to the host’s family, and
many a ten-cent piece did “ Bones ” thus yield to
the till. And the crazy piano at the far end of
the spacious, low-roofed room has probably dis-
appeared along with the anatomical specimen,
and the bears and monkeys, and the buffalo whose
tameness and love of “play” were such that he
used to career around his ptivate apartment like
an earthquake whenever you looked in to see him.
Only those who have gazed upon Joe Beef’s bar-
room piano can understand the depth of degrada-
tion to which a box of music may descend. There
was scarcely a suspicion of ivory left upon the
keys of the jingling instrument. The dirt
of many years had settled in its constitution, and
the name of its maker was indiscernible for
grease. In our mind’s eye we can picture Joe
Beef’s eldest son, a lad of 13 or so, at that time,
standing beside this same piano and singing, to
the accompaniment of a poor, wasted, consumptive
wretch, the popular ditty of the doss-house.
’Twas a long, descriptive songof many verses, and
the ad vantages offered to lodgers were explained
in the chorus :
There’s one cent for korfee,
And one cent for bread ; m
There’s three fora beefsteak,
And live for a lied,
And a breeze from the gutter—
A nice healthy smell
To refresh all the boys
At the Bummers’ Hotel.
Joe Beef acted religiously up to the terms of this
chorus. Vagrants coming into the Canteen in the
evening could cut their own steaks off the divided
carcase of a bullock, select half a loaf from a huge
cupboard full of them, and take their meal up-
stairs to be prepared by the cook. From a copper
in the middle of her “ kitchen ” the cook ladled
out a tin bowl of soup as a first course, and by the
time a man had “done” his soup, steak
and half loaf, and pannikin of korfee from
another copper, he was well filled for his five cents.
Then, if he liked, lie could turn at once into one
of the couple of hundred beds laid on the floors of
the neighbouring dormitories, and sleep till six in
the morning. At six o’clock Joe Beef struck a
gong, and his lodgers had to quit the show for the
day. The gong was the signal to get out. If they
were too tired or too drunk to wake up, Joe and
his henchman chucked them out promiscuously.
Sometimes it happened that a lodger was maimed,
or perhaps killed right away, by the chucking, but
this made no difference to anybody but the victim.
Joe Beef reigned as king of the waifs and strays of
Montreal, and he had a policeman’s license to
commit any small accidents of the kind. He was
more than a provider of beds and banquets at five
cents a head. He was the father of the destitute*
inasmuch as he allowed his wretched customers
unlimited “ tick,” if necessary. They could always
fly from starvation to the Bummers’ Hotel* pro-
vided that they made a decent pretence
of looking for work. Joe pitched them
forth at 6 a.m. in order that they might
make an early start in quest of a job*
The Harmy can change the old ramshackle Cart :
teen into a Food and Shelter Depot, but it will
never do any better for the outcasts of Montreal
than Joe Beef did for them. The Harmy may
tone down the colour of the language used in the
establishment, but at the same time it is tolerably
certain to tone down the dimensions of the steaks.
Any introduction of prayer amongst the “bum-
mers ” will not compensate for the loss of the big
brown bear that used to ruminate in a deep hole
under the kitchen, almost immediately below the
soup-copper. Joe had a rough-and-ready way of
converting a real hard case by opening the trap-
door of this hole and threatening to drop the
sinner upon the beast of prey. Moreover, the
influence of Joe’s wild philanthropy made itself
felt in the place, and was as much Christian reli-
gion as any poor devil could require—especially
after the snow had commenced to fall and sleighs
were running on the frozen St. Lawrence River
hard by. The writer, having the recollection of a
riotous night at Joe Beef’s engraved upon his
heart, begs to express his regret that General
Booth is the new landlord of this charitable insti-
tution. He will alter it out of knowledge, and
stop the supply of strong drink. Instead of “ One
cent for korfee and one cent for bread,” &c., they
will sing hymns and weary the lodgers with exhor-
tations to prepare for death Just as if there
oiuld be a more simple and practical reminder of
the future state than Joe Beef’s father’s skeleton.

A Protest.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A Melbourne journal reprints from a London organ a
paragraph, to the effect that, on a recent evening, the
Prince of Wales was seen to stop in the street and buy a
penny box of matches from an old man. And, gushingly
adds the paper, “ he paid for it.”
We have read with bilious vapours feeble bunkum
in the papers, the vilest kind of pappycock
that fires the wrath of men,
And we wish to say we’re weary of the stories
dull and dreary, with a weariness and loathing
far beyond our power to pen.
'Tis the paragraph infernal in the loyal local
journal, or the greasy rag devoted to “society”
affairs,
Re the prince who gave a tanner, in a condescend-
ing manner, to a kiddy on the highway, now
at which our soul despairs.
Again we feel our stomach turning when we meet
a par. concerning His Highness Albert
Edward and his pleasant, simple way,
Showing how he spoke benignly to a pauper or
divinely shed a smile upon an orphan, like a
man of couimyp clay.
When we read the Czar of Russia is a fatherly old
brusher—that he’ll push the p’rambulator, or
hold baby for a spell,
Or he’ll do a bovine gambol with the children, or
a ramble in the Woods to gather mushrooms —
We feel horribly unwell.
Then we’re moved to fits of madness by that
loathly chirp of gladness re some beauteous
British princess bathed in sympathetic tears,
Or her kindness to the peasants in accepting little
presents whilst a wretched crowd stand ’round
her and give idiotic cheers.
Much we hate that wayworn fable that they send
along per cable, informing us Her Gracious
heaves a sympathetic sigh
When she hears of inundations overwhelming
towns and stations—especially as we’ve always
got to settle for the lie.
We would slay the liar hoary, who concocts that
dreadful story of the stranger whom he meets
with, who is nice as anything,
Who is fairly level-pated and not badly educated,
and yet turns nut (wild amazement!) to be so-
and-so, the king.
Save ns from the tale of Tummy, to a drummer
bland and chummy, of the duke who carried
parcels, and who wasn’t proud or vain ;
Of his earlship, simple-minded, who once led a
beggar blinded o’er a crossing, or the noble
lord who “dresses very plain !”
There’s insanity quiescent in the man whom these
incessant debilitating items moves to raptures,
and who then
“ Loyal ” sentiments evinces, having learnt that
kings and princes, and dukes and earls, and
such like, on occasion act like men.
SILAS SNELL.
“Yon study three weeks, you love three
weeks, quarrel three years, suffer each other for
30 years, and your children go the same way.”—
Taine.
Capfc. Reeves, of the P. and O. steamer,
Australia, which has made the record run out
from London, claims that his vessel can be
steamed “ comfortably ” nearly a knot an hour
faster than any other Australian liner afloat, “not
excepting the Britannia, whose average is about
15.6.”
Victoria Woodhull and her sister Claflin
opened the - fashionable American restaurants to
the unfettered patronage of woman, without male
attendants. The chief incident in this campaign
was their going to Delmonico’s, where admittance
was refused them. They took in the first man
out of the street, and treated him to a tiptop
dinner.
Dr. Youll was “all there” in the Melbourne
coroner’s chair, but once he struck a snag. Re-
calcitrant and bibulous juryman; “I objeck to
this sort thing! These other ’leven fellers are
only bloomin’ sticks—take all you shay for gospel!
Verdiek not worth a tinker’s damn !” Coroner,
with immense dignity : “Sir, if you come here to
sit on a jury you must conduct yourself in a
becoming manner.” “Come here? Dash it all, I
—hie—didn’t come here ! I was fetched here —
yanked along against my will by a—hie—copper!”
And Youll “gave him best.”
The other day English Labour-member
Keir Hardie, referring to his well-known flannel
collar, said his wife wished him to wear white
collars like other people, but he found dark flannel
ones more comfortable and convenient and less
costly. “ Like other people!” Here one sees the
beginning of a socio-political tragedy. Observe
the woman getting in the thin edge of the wedge
of Fashion that leadeth to Esq., a silk hat, broad-
cloth and—a title. Unhappy the woman whose
Labour-member husband will only wear a brown
flannel shirt and a tweed cap !
An Auckland (M.L.) man complains that
the law over there is altogether too incomprehen-
sible. He was employed by a small firm, and had
to sue for his wages, but the lawyer on the other
side demonstrated to of the Court
that he wasn’t an but a partner, and
therefore not entitled to wages, and the case was
dismissed with costs. Then he sued, as a partner,
for his share of the firm’s profits, and the same
lawyer got up and demonstrated to the satisfac-
tion of the same Court that he wasn’t a partner,
but only an and therefore not entitled to
profits, and the second case was dismissed with
costs. Last of all, he wrote to the Law Institute,
asking the opinion of that body as to whether the
lawyer in question wasn’t a liar, but so far he has
received no answer.

The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause,' and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of nationa
progress; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the clahns of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital’ is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
tnat so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.

LIFE IS SHORT!
SO VERY SHORT!
What a pity we cannot have a Lease of Life!
Townspeople require a trip—ruralise in the country. Country folk, on
the other hand, improve their health by a change of air—sea air, city air,
Sydtiey air—and such is life. Still, how much better to study one’s health,
and live so long as we can. One and all should make the best of this
planet. Even an occasional trip to Sydney does one a world of good, and
the cost is but a trifle. Now, good husband, if your wife be ill or unsettled,
send her straight away to Sydney. Probably, she will inquire to spend a
few pounds in house and family requisites, in little necessary articles men
never dream of, and should know naught about, merely simple little things
which all good women require to adorn and make home what a home should
be -a paradise of its kind. And, again, little nic-nacs—Boots, etc.—for
the children are wanted. Nice, tidy fashions to decorate your dear chicks,
and make them happy. Make your boys ambitious, iff a good plan. Your
son may some day hold Duffs or Dibbs’ position ; every boy has his chance
in this lottery of life.
We are now coming to business. I am FOY, good sir, and I vow to
you your wife will save surely Four Pounds in every outlay of Ten Pounds
in DRAPERY, BOOTS and SHOES, etc.- that is, by coming and dealing
with your humble servant, Foy. Foy is a little, fat, unpretentious haber-
dashery man a draper who is not proud, but truly loves his trade.
Foy never deceives or cheats. Foy feels most proud and contented
when the public praise his shop, his goods, his assistants, his prices, and
admire his endeavours to please. Say ! Foy is not a bad man.
OXFORD STREET, SYDNEY
(Nrar HYDE PARK).
MARK FOY’S.
A PROPHECY.
DON’T BE DISTRESSED. Depression will soon give place to prosperity by thoughtful economy
and good laws. The lands of the colony should be its source of wealth, but this can only be
practicable by a profitable market being provided for the products of the land. The railway rates
should be adapted, somewhat upon the Zone system, to the wants of the inland districts. By carrying
rates to the sea-board, so that the tillers of the soil may have a profitable market provided (America
owes her great prosperity mainly to this), emigration would then flow into the country, population
increase, and a natural boom the result.
